Past Nafaqah Requirement: Qadi Fixing or Settlement
Ruling that past nafaqah is not owed unless qadi fixed it or settlement made

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on past nafaqah and explain why it does not become a debt without a qadi's fixing or settlement.

Core Idea
Past nafaqah is not automatically owed; it must be fixed by a qadi or settled to become binding.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi family law: past nafaqah does not accumulate as a debt automatically. The ruling is based on the categorization of nafaqah as a gift (silah) rather than compensation (iwad). We will explore the reasoning and the two exceptions where past nafaqah becomes due: qadi's judgment or mutual settlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The base ruling: if a period passes without nafaqah, the wife gets nothing for the past.
This is the default position according to our school.
2
Recognize the two exceptions
Past nafaqah is owed if (1) the qadi had previously fixed the nafaqah amount, or (2) the couple reached a settlement (sulh) regarding the amount.
Sulh is effective because the husband has authority over himself.
3
Understand the legal basis
Nafaqah is classified as silah (gift), not iwad (compensation). This means obligation is not firmly established until a qadi's ruling or settlement creates it, analogous to a gift (hibah) requiring possession (qabd).
This classification is specific to Hanafi fiqh.
4
Contrast with mahr
Mahr is iwad (compensation), so it becomes an immediate debt upon marriage. Nafaqah is different.
The distinction highlights the importance of the silah/iwad binary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا مضت مدة لم ينفق الزوج عليها وطالبته بذالك فلاشئ لها الا ان يكون القاضى فرض لها النفقة او صالحت الزوج على مقدار نفقتها فيقضى لها بنفقة ما مضى لان النفقة صلة وليست بعوض عندنا على مامر من قبل فلا يستحكم الوجوب فيها الا بالقضاء كالهبة لاتوجب الملك الابموكد وهو القبض والصلح بمنزلة القضاء لان ولايته على نفسه اقوى من ولاية القاضي بخلاف المهرلانــه عـوض
ترجمہ اور اگر ایک مدت گزر گئی کہ شوہر نے بیوی کو نفقہ نہیں دیا۔ پھر بیوی نے اس سے گذشتہ نفقہ کا مطالبہ کیا۔ تو عورت کیلئے کچھ نہیں الا یہ کہ قاضی نے مقرر کر دیا ہو یا کسی مقدار پر مصالحت کر دی ہو تو اس صورت میں گذشتہ نفقہ اسے ملے گا کیونکہ نفقہ ہمارے نزدیک صِلہ ( گفٹ ) ہے اور عوض نہیں جیسا کہ پہلے گذر چکا ہے۔ اس لئے نفقہ کے وجوب میں قاضی کے فیصلہ سے پہلے استحکام نہیں ہوتا۔ جیسا کہ ہبہ کے بغیر قبضہ کے ملک ثابت نہیں ہوتا۔ اور صلح قاضی کے فیصلہ کے درجہ میں ہے۔ کیونکہ شوہر کا اپنے اوپر ولایت قاضی کی ولایت سے زیادہ قوی ہے۔ برخلاف مہر کے کہ وہ عوض ہے۔
'And if a period passed in which the husband did not spend on her, and she demanded that from him, then there is nothing for her, unless the qadi fixed nafaqah for her, or she made a settlement with the husband for the amount of her nafaqah, then he will judge for her the nafaqah of what passed, because nafaqah is a gift and not a compensation according to us, as has preceded. So the obligation is not firmly established except by judgment, like a gift: it does not cause ownership except by taking possession. And a settlement is like a judgment because his authority over himself is stronger than the authority of the qadi. Contrary to mahr, because it is a compensation.' Translation: And if a period passed in which the husband did not give nafaqah to his wife, then the wife demanded past nafaqah from him, then nothing is for her, unless the qadi fixed it or she made a settlement for a certain amount, then in that case she will receive the past nafaqah. Because according to us, nafaqah is a gift (silah) and not a compensation (iwad), as has passed earlier. Therefore, before the qadi's decision, the obligation is not firm. Just as a gift does not establish ownership without taking possession (qabd). And a settlement is in the rank of a qadi's judgment because the husband's authority over himself is stronger than the qadi's authority. Unlike mahr, because it is a compensation.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the entitlement to past nafaqah. According to the Hanafi school, if a husband fails to pay nafaqah for a past period, the wife cannot claim it retroactively unless the qadi had previously fixed the nafaqah or the couple had reached a settlement (sulh) regarding the amount. The reason is that nafaqah, according to the Hanafi view, is considered a gift (silah) rather than a compensation (iwad). As a gift, it does not become a firmly established debt (like mahr) until a qadi's judgment or an agreement creates obligation. This is analogous to a gift (hibah) which does not transfer ownership without acceptance and possession (qabd). The text also explains that the husband's own settlement (sulh) is equivalent to a qadi's ruling because his authority over himself (wilayah 'ala nafsihi) is stronger than the qadi's authority. This contrasts with mahr, which is a compensation and thus becomes a debt immediately upon marriage. The ruling highlights the Hanafi principle that nafaqah is a recurring obligation that does not automatically accumulate as a debt for past periods; it must be actively fixed by a judge or voluntarily agreed upon.

Key Terms
صلة (silah): A gift or gratuitous transfer; nafaqah is categorized as silah, meaning it is not a compensatory obligation like a debt.
عوض (iwad): Compensation; something given in exchange for something else. Mahr is an iwad for the marriage contract.
صلح (sulh): Settlement or compromise between parties; here it refers to the husband and wife agreeing on past nafaqah amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is sufficient to make past nafaqah owed in Hanafi law?
A) The husband's mere promise to pay
B) A prior qadi's fixing of nafaqah
C) The wife's demand letter
D) A court case filed after the period

Key Takeaway
Past nafaqah is not automatically a debt in Hanafi law; it requires a qadi's fixing or a settlement to become binding, because nafaqah is classified as a gift (silah).

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Adjusting Nafaqah When Husband's Wealth Changes
Ruling and proof for adjusting nafaqah when husband's financial status changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adjusting nafaqah after a qadi's decree and explain the proof that nafaqah is not a fixed debt but a daily accruing obligation.

Core Idea
The qadi's nafaqah decree is only an estimation for future, not a binding debt, so it adjusts with the husband's financial status.

When a qadi sets nafaqah based on the husband's poverty, then the husband becomes wealthy, should the wife accept the lower amount? The Hanafi school says no: nafaqah is a recurring obligation that changes with the husband's ability. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its rational proof from the Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage rules: if a qadi initially awards nafaqah based on the husband's insolvency (i'sar), and later the husband becomes wealthy (yasar), the wife can demand the full nafaqah of a wealthy husband. The qadi must award the higher amount.
The ruling applies even though a prior qadi decree exists.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): 'al-nafaqah takhtalif bihasab al-yasar wa al-i'sar' — nafaqah varies with wealth and poverty. Also, the qadi's decree is 'taqdir li-nafaqah lam tajib' — an estimation for a nafaqah not yet due.
No explicit Quran or hadith; the ruling is derived from the nature of nafaqah as a daily maintenance.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah (Legal Import)
The phrase 'taqdir li-nafaqah lam tajib' shows the qadi's order is not a binding debt but a provisional estimate for future periods. Since each day's nafaqah becomes due separately, the cause (husband's ability) can change, allowing adjustment.
This distinguishes nafaqah from fixed debts where a qadi's decree is final.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf (Disagreement)
The Shafi'i school holds that once the qadi fixes nafaqah, it becomes a binding debt even if the husband's status changes. Their proof: the qadi's decree is a final judgment.
Hanafis rebut by emphasizing the nature of nafaqah as a recurring grant (silah), not a one-time debt.
5
Identify the Tarjih (Preponderance)
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) in the school, as recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah. The passage explicitly endorses this view, and later Hanafi works follow it.
The Shafi'i view is recognized but not adopted in Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب النفقة
واذا قضى القاضي لها بنفقة الاعسار ثم ايسر فخاصمته تمم لها نفقة الموسرلان النفقة تختلف بحسب اليسار والاعسار و ما قضى به تقدير لنفقة لم تجب فاذا تبدل حاله لها المطالبة بتمام حقها
ترجمہ اور اگر قاضی نے عورت کے واسطے تنگی کا نفقہ مقرر کر دیا پھر (اس کا شوہر ) مالدار ہو گیا۔ پس عورت نے اس سے مخاصمت کی تو قاضی اس کے لئے خوشحالی کا نفقہ پورا کر دیگا۔ کیونکہ فراخی اور تنگی کے موافق نفقہ بھی مختلف ہوتا ہے۔ اور قاضی نے حکم دیا وہ ایسے نفقہ کے واسطے اندازہ ہے جو ابھی واجب نہیں ہوا پس جب شوہر کا حال بدل گیا۔ تو بیوی کیلئے اپنے پورے حق کے مطالبہ کا اختیار ہے۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر قاضی نے کسی عورت کے واسطے تنگی اور غربت کا نفقہ فرض کیا پھر اس کا شوہر خوشحال ہو گیا۔ پس عورت نے قاضی کے پاس خوشحالی کے نفقہ کا دعویٰ کیا۔ تو قاضی اس کیلئے خوشحالی کا نفقہ مقرر کر دے گا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ فراخی اور تنگی کے موافق نفقہ بدلتا رہتا ہے۔ کیونکہ نفقہ پوری زندگی کیلئے یکبارگی واجب نہیں ہوتا۔ بلکہ ہر روز تھوڑا تھوڑا واجب ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے ہر دن شوہر اور بیوی کے حال کا اعتبار کیا جائے گا۔
ومــاقـضــی بـه تقدیر سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ اگر عورت کیلئے تنگی کا نفقہ مقرر کر دیا۔ پھر شوہر مالدار ہو گیا تو عورت کے واسطے مالداری کا نفقہ پورا نہ کرنا چاہئے۔ کیونکہ ایسا کرنے میں قاضی کا پہلا فیصلہ باطل ہو جائے گا۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ قاضی نے جو حکم دیا ہے وہ ایسے نفقہ کے واسطے اندازہ ہے۔ جو ابھی تک واجب نہیں ہوا۔ اس لئے کہ ہر روز کا نفقہ تھوڑا تھوڑا واجب ہوتا ہے۔ اور جو چیز ابھی تک واجب نہیں ہوئی اس کا اندازہ بھی لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ممکن ہے کہ اس کے وجوب سے پہلے سبب جو واجب کرنے والا ہے وہ بدل جائے ۔ پس جب قاضی کا مقرر کیا ہوا انداز ولازم نہیں ہوا تو اس کا فیصلہ بھی مستحکم اور مضبوط نہیں ہے۔ اور جب قاضی کا فیصلہ مستحکم نہیں تو اس کا باطل کرنا بھی لازم نہیں آیا ۔ لہذا جب شوہر کا حال بدل گیا تو عورت اپنے پورے حق کا مطالبہ کر سکتی ہے۔
Chapter of Nafaqah
And if the qadi decreed for her nafaqah of insolvency, then (the husband) became wealthy, and she disputed (with him), he will complete for her the nafaqah of the wealthy, because nafaqah varies according to wealth and insolvency. And what he decreed is an estimation for a nafaqah that has not become obligatory. So when his situation changes, she has the right to demand her full right.
Translation: And if the qadi fixed for the woman the nafaqah of hardship, then her husband became wealthy. Then the woman disputed with him, so the qadi will complete for her the nafaqah of prosperity. Because nafaqah varies according to ease and hardship. And the qadi's order is an estimation for a nafaqah that has not yet become obligatory. So when the husband's situation changed, the wife has the option to demand her full right.
Explanation: The issue is that if the qadi fixed for a woman the nafaqah of hardship and poverty, then her husband became prosperous. Then the woman claimed the nafaqah of prosperity before the qadi. So the qadi will fix for her the nafaqah of prosperity. The proof is that nafaqah changes according to ease and hardship. Because nafaqah does not become obligatory all at once for the entire life, but little by little each day. Therefore each day the condition of the husband and wife will be considered.
And 'what he decreed is an estimation' is an answer to a question. The question is: If the nafaqah of hardship was fixed for the woman, then the husband became wealthy, then the nafaqah of wealth should not be completed for her, because doing so would nullify the qadi's first decision. The answer is: What the qadi ordered is an estimation for a nafaqah that has not yet become obligatory. Because each day's nafaqah becomes obligatory little by little. And something that has not yet become obligatory, its estimation is also not binding, because it is possible that before its obligation, the cause that obligates it changes. So when the qadi's fixed estimation did not become binding, his decision is also not firm and strong. And when the qadi's decision is not firm, nullifying it is also not necessary. Therefore, when the husband's situation changed, the woman can demand her full right.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah. The issue: a qadi initially awards nafaqah based on the husband's insolvency (اعسار). Later the husband becomes wealthy. The wife claims the higher nafaqah of the wealthy. The ruling: the qadi will award the higher amount. The proof: nafaqah varies with wealth and poverty, and it is not a one-time obligation but accrues daily. Therefore, each day's obligation depends on the current status. The passage then addresses a potential objection: does this not nullify the qadi's first decree? The answer: the qadi's decree was only an estimation for future nafaqah that had not yet become due. Since it was not a final binding debt, it can be modified when circumstances change. This is a reminder that nafaqah is not like a fixed debt but a recurring maintenance that adjusts.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance and financial support that a husband must provide to his wife, including food, clothing, and shelter.
اعسار (i'sar): State of financial hardship or insolvency, where the husband cannot afford the full nafaqah.
قضا (qada): Judicial decree or order of a qadi regarding nafaqah.

Past Nafaqah: When Does It Become Binding?
Ruling with proof on past nafaqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on past nafaqah and identify the conditions under which it becomes enforceable.

Core Idea
Past nafaqah is not a debt unless a qadi decreed or the spouses settled—nafaqah is a grant, not compensation.

In the Hanafi school, a wife's claim for past nafaqah is not automatically granted. The ruling hinges on whether the obligation was made firm by a judge's decree or a mutual settlement. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the cross-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for past nafaqah
Identify the default ruling: if a husband fails to pay nafaqah, the wife gets nothing for the past period unless exceptions apply.
The rule is based on the nature of nafaqah as a grant (silah).
2
Identify the two exceptions
Find the two conditions that make past nafaqah enforceable: a qadi's decree fixing the amount, or a mutual settlement (sulh) between spouses.
Settlement is even stronger than a decree because the husband has authority over himself.
3
Understand the proof: analogy to hibah
Examine why nafaqah is not automatically a debt: it is a silah, not 'iwad. It is analogized to a gift (hibah) which requires possession (qabd) to bind.
The analogy is qiyas jaliy.
4
Contrast with mahr
Note the contrast with mahr, which is compensation and becomes a debt immediately without need for decree.
Mahr is 'iwad, silah is not.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i school disagrees; they hold nafaqah is a debt immediately. Understand the practical consequence.
The difference stems from the nature of nafaqah.

Source Text

ترجمہ اور اگر ایک مدت گزر گئی کہ شوہر نے بیوی کو نفقہ نہیں دیا۔ پھر بیوی نے اس سے گذشتہ نفقہ کا مطالبہ کیا۔ تو عورت کیلئے کچھ نہیں ہوگا۔ مگر یہ کہ قاضی نے اس کے لئے نفقہ کی کوئی مقدار ) فرض کی ہو۔ یا اس نے اپنے شوہر نے اپنے نفقہ کی کسی مقدار پر صلح کرلی ہو۔ تو (اسی حساب سے ) قاضی اس کے لئے گذشتہ نفقہ کا حکم دے گا۔ کیونکہ نفقہ تو عطیہ ہے۔ اور ہمارے نزدیک ( ملک کا ) عوض نہیں ہے۔ چنانچہ سابق میں بیان ہو چکا۔ پس نفقہ میں واجب ہونا مستحکم نہیں ہوتا۔ مگر جب کہ قاضی کا حکم ہو جائے جیسے ہبہ ملک واجب نہیں کرتا مگر (جبکہ ) مضبوط کرنے والی چیز یعنی قبضہ (پایا جائے ) اور عورت کا مرد کے ساتھ کسی چیز پر صلح کرنا حکم قاضی کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ کیونکہ شوہر کی ولایت اپنی ذات پر قاضی کی ولایت سے بڑھ کر ہے۔ بخلاف مہر کے اس لئے کہ وہ عوض ہوتا ہے۔
تشریح مسلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک مدت گذرگئی اور شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کو نفقہ نہیں دیا۔ پھر اس نے اپنے شوہر سے اس مدت کے نفقہ کا مطالبہ کیا تو ہمارے نزدیک بیوی کو کچھ نہیں ملے گا۔ کیونکہ مدت گذر جانے کی وجہ سے نفقہ شوہر کے ذمہ قرضہ نہیں ہوتا۔ پس جب گذشتہ مدت کا نفقہ شوہر کے ذمہ قرضہ نہیں ہے۔ تو قاضی گذشتہ نفقہ کا حکم بھی نہیں دے گا۔ مگر دو صورتیں اس حکم سے مستفی ہیں۔ ایک یہ کہ قاضی نے عورت کے واسطے نفقہ کی کوئی مقدار مقرر کی ہو۔ دوم یہ کہ عورت نے اپنے شوہر سے نفقہ کی کسی مقدار پر صلح کر لی ہو۔ ان دونوں صورتوں میں اسی حساب سے قاضی عورت کے لئے گذشتہ کے نفقہ کا حکم دیدے گا۔
دلیل یہ ہے کہ نفقہ عطیہ ہے اور ہمارے نزدیک ملک کا عوض نہیں، جیسا کہ سابق میں گذر چکا لہذا نفقہ کا وجوب مستحکم اور مضبوط نہیں ہو گا۔ مگر جب کہ قاضی کا حکم ہو جائے۔ جیسے ہبہ کی صورت میں موہوب لہ کیلئے اس وقت ملک ثابت ہوگی جبکہ مضبوط کرنے والی چیز پر قبضہ پایا جائے ۔ اسی طرح نفقہ کا موہوب قاضی کے حکم سے مستحکم اور مضبوط ہوگا اور عورت کا اپنے شوہر کے ساتھ کسی چیز پر صلح کرنا ایسا ہے جیسا که قاضی کا حکم کرنا۔ کیونکہ شوہر کی ولایت اپنی ذات پر قاضی کی ولایت سے بڑھ کر ہے۔ اس لئے کہ نفقہ کی جو مقدار قاضی نے مقرر کی ہے شوہر اگر چاہے تو اس زائد کا التزام کر سکتا ہے۔ پس صلح بمنزلہ قضاء قاضی کے ہے بلکہ اس سے بھی بڑھ کر بر خلاف مہر کے کیونکہ وہ ملک بضع کا عوض ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے وہ بغیر قاضی کے حکم کے لازم ہو جائے گا۔
And if a period passed during which the husband did not spend (nafaqah) on her, and she demanded that from him, then there is nothing for her, unless the qadi prescribed nafaqah for her, or she made a settlement with the husband on an amount of her nafaqah. Then he will decree for her the nafaqah of what passed, because nafaqah is a grant (silah) and not compensation (iwad) according to us, as has preceded. So the obligation does not become firm except by a judicial decree, like a gift (hibah) does not cause ownership except by a firm act, which is taking possession (qabd). And settlement (sulh) is in the position of judicial decree, because his authority over himself is stronger than the authority of the qadi, unlike mahr because it is compensation.
Translation: And if a period passed that the husband did not give nafaqah to the wife, then the wife demanded past nafaqah from him, then nothing is due to the woman, unless the qadi fixed an amount of nafaqah for her, or she made a settlement with her husband on an amount of her nafaqah. Then accordingly the qadi will decree for her the past nafaqah. Because nafaqah is a gift, and according to us it is not compensation for ownership. As has been explained before. So obligation in nafaqah does not become firm except by a judge's order, like hibah does not necessitate ownership except by a strengthening thing, which is possession. And the woman's settlement with the man for something is in the rank of the judge's order, because the husband's authority over his own self is stronger than the judge's authority. Unlike mahr, because it is compensation.
Explanation: The issue is that if a period passed and the husband did not give nafaqah to his wife, then she demanded nafaqah for that period, then in our view the wife gets nothing, because the passing of time does not make nafaqah a debt upon the husband. So when past nafaqah is not a debt, the qadi will not decree it. But two cases are exempted from this ruling: (1) the qadi had fixed an amount of nafaqah for the woman, (2) the woman made a settlement with her husband on an amount of nafaqah. In these two cases, the qadi will decree past nafaqah accordingly.
The proof is that nafaqah is a gift, and according to us it is not compensation for ownership, as has passed. Therefore the obligation of nafaqah does not become firm except by a judicial decree. Like hibah: ownership is established for the donee only when a strengthening element, possession, is present. Similarly, nafaqah becomes firm by the qadi's decree. And the woman's settlement with her husband is like the qadi's decree, because the husband's authority over his own self is stronger than the qadi's authority. This is because the qadi's fixed amount can be increased by the husband if he wishes. So settlement is like the qadi's decree, even stronger. Unlike mahr, because it is compensation for the right of sexual intercourse, so it becomes binding without a judicial decree.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about past (madi) nafaqah. The general rule: If a period elapses during which the husband did not provide nafaqah, and the wife later claims it, she gets nothing. Why? Because nafaqah in the Hanafi school is a 'silah' (grant) not an 'iwad' (exchange). It does not become a binding debt on the husband merely by the passage of time. The obligation is only established and made firm by a qadi's decree or by a settlement (sulh) between the spouses. The analogy is made with hibah (gift): a gift does not transfer ownership until possession (qabd) is taken. Similarly, nafaqah becomes a binding obligation only when the qadi orders it. However, there are two exceptions: (1) if the qadi had already fixed the amount of nafaqah for the wife (even if not yet collected), then past amounts become due; (2) if the wife and husband settled on a specific amount of nafaqah, then that settlement is even stronger than a qadi's decree because the husband has authority over himself. This is contrasted with mahr (dower), which is a compensation and thus becomes a binding debt immediately, without need for qadi's decree.

Key Terms
صلہ (silah): A grant or gift, not given in exchange for something else.
صلح (sulh): Settlement or mutual agreement between the spouses on the amount of nafaqah.
ہبہ (hibah): Gift; transfer of ownership without exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only if the husband admits he did not pay.

Key Takeaway
Past nafaqah is not a debt; it is only enforceable if a qadi fixed it or the spouses settled on an amount.

Death of Spouse After Nafaqah Decree: Grant or Debt?
Inter-madhhab dispute on falling of nafaqah after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the effect of death on decreed nafaqah and explain the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Nafaqah decreed but unpaid falls upon death because it is a grant, not a debt.

This passage from Al-Hidayah examines a critical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the legal nature of nafaqah. When a qadi decrees maintenance but the wife has not yet collected it, and one spouse dies, does the obligation persist or vanish? The answer depends on whether nafaqah is a grant or a compensation. Let us dissect the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: nafaqah falls (saqit) by death of either spouse after decree but before collection.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that nafaqah becomes a debt (dayn) upon decree and does not fall by death.
Same as asking which school objects.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i considers nafaqah as iwad (exchange) for the wife's tamkin (availability), analogous to other compensations like mahr.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response, referenced as previously explained, is that nafaqah is a silah (grant), not an exchange. Like a gift (hibah) before possession, it is voided by death.
This response is based on qiyas to hibah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In Hanafi, no claim from the estate; in Shafi'i, the estate must pay the decreed nafaqah.
This is the key difference in application.

And likewise if the wife dies, because nafaqah is a grant, and grants fall by death, like a gift (hibah) becomes void by death before possession. Because nafaqah is a gift, and gifts fall due to death, just as a gift (mawhub) becomes void by death before possession. Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the effect of death on nafaqah that has been decreed by a qadi. The Hanafi position: nafaqah is a 'silah' (grant) not an 'iwad' (exchange). Therefore, like a gift that has not yet been possessed, it is extinguished by the death of either party. So if the husband dies after the decree but before the wife actually takes the money, the obligation falls. The same if the wife dies. Al-Shafi'i's position: nafaqah is an 'iwad' (compensation for the wife's availability), so once decreed, it becomes a binding debt like any other, and death does not extinguish it; the heirs must pay it. The text states that the Hanafi response has already been given earlier (reference to the analogy with hibah). The passage emphasizes the Hanafi view that nafaqah's nature as a grant prevents it from being a fixed debt.

Key Terms
صلہ (silah): A grant or benevolent gift, not given in exchange for something.
عوض (iwad): Compensation or exchange; something given in return for a benefit.
سقوط (suqut): Falling or extinguishment of an obligation due to death or other cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It becomes a debt on the husband's estate and must be paid.
B) It falls (is extinguished) because nafaqah is a grant, not a debt.
C) It is reduced by half.
D) It is transferred to the wife's inheritance share.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, decreed nafaqah not yet paid falls upon death because it is a grant, not a debt.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Past Nafaqah Not Due as Debt Unless Fixed by Qadi or Settlement
Ruling on past nafaqah with exceptions and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on past nafaqah and identify the two exceptions that make it enforceable.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is a gift, not a debt — past payments require qadi or settlement to be firm.

In the Hanafi school, maintenance (nafaqah) is considered a gift (ʿaṭiyyah) rather than compensation (ʿiwaḍ). This classification has a direct practical consequence: if a husband fails to pay maintenance for a past period, that amount does not automatically become a debt. However, there are two exceptions that can make it enforceable. This lesson explains the ruling, the exceptions, and the legal reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State that past nafaqah does not become a debt upon the husband merely by the passage of time.
This is the default Hanafi position.
2
Recognize the two exceptions
List the two cases where past nafaqah can be claimed: (1) when a qadi previously fixed an amount, or (2) when the couple settled (ṣulḥ) on a specific sum.
3
Understand the legal basis
Explain the analogy to hibah (gift): a gift does not confer ownership without possession; similarly, nafaqah does not become firm without a qadi's order or equivalent.
This is the core qiyas (analogical reasoning) supporting the ruling.
4
Contrast with mahr
Note that mahr is compensation (ʿiwaḍ) and therefore becomes binding immediately without qadi's ruling.
This contrast highlights the unique nature of nafaqah.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a case where a wife demands past nafaqah, determine whether she can claim it based on whether a qadi had fixed it or settlement occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

But if the qadi has fixed a certain amount of maintenance for her, or she and her husband have settled on a certain amount of maintenance, then (accordingly) the qadi will order past maintenance for her. Because maintenance is a gift, and in our view it is not a compensation (for ownership). Thus it was previously explained. So the obligation of maintenance is not firm, except when the qadi has ruled, just as hibah does not necessitate ownership except (when) the strengthening thing, i.e., possession (is found). And a woman settling with her husband on something is equivalent to the qadi's ruling, because the husband's authority over his own self is greater than the qadi's authority. Unlike mahr, because it is compensation. Explanation of the issue: If a period passed and the husband did not give his wife maintenance, then she demands from her husband the maintenance for that period, then in our view the wife will get nothing. Because due to the elapse of the period, maintenance does not become a debt upon the husband. So when the past period's maintenance is not a debt upon the husband, then the qadi will not order past maintenance either. But two cases are excepted from this ruling. One: the qadi fixed a certain amount of maintenance for the woman. Second: the woman settled with her husband on a certain amount of maintenance. In both these cases, accordingly, the qadi will order past maintenance for the woman. The proof is that maintenance is a gift, and in our view it is not compensation for ownership, as has passed earlier. Hence the obligation of maintenance is not firm and strong, except when the qadi's ruling occurs. Just as in the case of hibah, ownership is established for the donee when possession, the strengthening thing, is found. Similarly, the maintenance becomes firm and strong by the qadi's ruling. And a woman's settlement with her husband is like the qadi's ruling, because the husband's authority over his own self is greater than the qadi's authority. This is because the amount of maintenance that the qadi fixed, the husband, if he wishes, can undertake more. So settlement is in the position of the qadi's judgment, rather even stronger. Unlike mahr, because it is compensation for ownership of the private part. Therefore it becomes binding without the qadi's ruling.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on past (māḍī) maintenance (nafaqah). The basic rule is that if a period has elapsed without the husband paying maintenance, the wife cannot later claim it as a debt unless the qadi had previously fixed the amount or the couple settled on a specific sum. The reason is that nafaqah is classified as a gift (ʿaṭiyyah), not as a compensation (ʿiwaḍ) for the wife's confinement (iḥtibās). Since it is a gift, its obligation is not firmly established until either a qadi's ruling gives it legal force or the husband's own undertaking (through ṣulḥ) strengthens it. The analogy is drawn to hibah (gift): a gift does not confer ownership until possession (qabḍ) is taken. Similarly, nafaqah does not become an enforceable debt until the qadi's order or equivalent. The husband's settlement is considered stronger than the qadi's order because the husband has greater authority over himself and can voluntarily increase the amount. This contrasts with mahr (dower), which is a compensation for the right to sexual enjoyment and therefore becomes binding immediately without need for a qadi's ruling. The passage thus clarifies that past nafaqah is not automatically owed; it requires formal determination to become a firm liability.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance due from husband to wife, covering food, clothing, shelter, etc.
عوض (ʿiwaḍ): Compensation or counter-value in exchange for something.
صلح (ṣulḥ): Settlement or compromise between husband and wife regarding the amount of maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does past nafaqah become a firm obligation that can be claimed?
A) As soon as the period ends
B) Only when the qadi fixes it or the couple settles on an amount
C) Only if the wife demands it in court
D) Only if the husband acknowledges it

Key Takeaway
Past nafaqah is not a debt unless a qadi fixed it or the couple settled; this stems from its classification as a gift rather than compensation.

Nafaqah After Qadi's Ruling: Does Death Cancel It?
Inter-madhhab dispute on nafaqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a qadi-ordered nafaqah falls with the death of either spouse, and contrast this with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is a gift, not a debt; once the husband or wife dies, it falls.

In this lesson, we examine a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the nature of nafaqah (maintenance). The Hanafi school considers nafaqah a pure gift (ṣilah), which is extinguished by the death of either spouse before actual payment. The Shafi'i school sees it as compensation (ʿiwaḍ) for the wife's sexual availability, making it a debt that survives death. We will explore the proofs and the underlying usul principles that lead to these divergent rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What does the Hanafi school rule regarding nafaqah after a qadi's ruling and death of either spouse?
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school disagrees and on what grounds? Identify the Shafi'i position.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is the Shafi'i dalil for considering nafaqah a debt?
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the Shafi'i argument? What is the key distinction regarding compensation?
The Hanafi response: compensation for sexual availability is exclusively mahr, so nafaqah is not a debt but a gift.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in application between the two schools?
In Hanafi law, the wife cannot claim the nafaqah from the estate; in Shafi'i law, she can.

And if the husband dies after nafaqah was ordered against him and months pass, the nafaqah falls. And likewise if the wife dies, because nafaqah is a gift (ṣilah), and gifts fall due to death, just as a hibah becomes void due to death before possession. Similarly if the wife died, it also falls. The proof is that nafaqah is a gift, and such gifts fall due to death. Just as if someone gifted something and before the donee takes possession, the donor died or the donee died, the gift becomes void. And Imam Shafi'i said that even before the qadi's ruling, nafaqah becomes a debt upon the husband and does not fall with his death; rather the wife will receive it from the husband's estate. His proof is that in his view, nafaqah is compensation for the ownership of the private part. Therefore it will be analogized to other debts, just as debts do not fall with the death of the debtor, similarly the wife's nafaqah will not fall with the husband's death. The author of al-Hidayah says: We have answered this earlier, i.e., under the topic 'If she is a minor who cannot be cohabited with, there is no maintenance for her,' it was stated that the compensation for ownership of the private part is mahr, and one thing does not have two compensations. Therefore nafaqah is not compensation for ownership of the private part.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the effect of death on an already-ordered nafaqah. The Hanafi position is that once a qadi has ordered nafaqah, if either the husband or wife dies before the payment is made, the obligation of nafaqah falls (sāqiṭ). The reasoning is that nafaqah is a pure gift (ṣilah), and gifts are extinguished by the death of either party, analogous to a hibah (gift) that has not yet been possessed. The Shāfiʿī school, however, argues that nafaqah becomes a debt (dayn) even before the qadi's ruling, and debts do not fall with death; rather, the wife may claim it from the husband's estate. The Shāfiʿī basis is that nafaqah is compensation (ʿiwaḍ) for the wife's sexual availability (tamkīn). The Ḥanafī response, cited from earlier in al-Hidāyah, is that the compensation for sexual availability is exclusively the mahr (dower); one right cannot have two compensations. Thus nafaqah is not a compensation and retains its nature as a gift. The Ḥanafī position is that even after a qadi's order, the nafaqah does not become a debt unless the qadi specifically orders the wife to take it as a debt. Absent such an order, death cancels the obligation.

Key Terms
صلة (ṣilah): A gift or gratuity, not given as compensation but as a benevolent act.
ساقط (sāqiṭ): Fallen, void, or extinguished; used for obligations that lapse under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the nafaqah obligation if the husband dies after the qadi's ruling but before payment?
A) The nafaqah becomes a debt and must be paid from the estate
B) The nafaqah falls and is not paid
C) The wife is entitled to half the amount
D) The nafaqah continues until the end of the iddah period

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, nafaqah remains a gift until actually paid; death of either spouse extinguishes it, unlike debts.

Prepaid Nafaqah: Recovery on Husband's Death Disputed
Intra-Hanafi dispute with inter-madhhab alignment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on recovering advance nafaqah after the husband's death, and explain the underlying analogy (gift vs. compensation).

Core Idea
Advance nafaqah: a gift not returned after death, or compensation that must be adjusted?

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf on one side, and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other, regarding the effect of the husband's death on prepaid annual nafaqah. The disagreement turns on the legal classification of the advance payment: is it a pure gift (ṣilah) or a compensation (ʿiwaḍ) for the wife's ongoing confinement? Understanding this ikhtilaf requires you to analyze the reasoning ('illah) and the analogies (qiyas) employed by each side.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed case
The husband gives his wife a full year's nafaqah in advance, then dies before the year ends. Does the wife keep the entire amount, or must she return the portion for the remaining months?
The dispute only arises if the wife has already taken possession of the money.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They say nothing is recovered. Their evidence: the advance is a gift (ṣilah); possession has been taken; gifts are not returned after death because their legal effect is complete. Analogous to hibah. Also, if the money perishes without consumption, there is consensus no return.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State the position of Muhammad (and al-Shafi'i)
Muhammad says calculate the nafaqah for the elapsed time; the wife keeps that, the rest goes to the husband's heirs. His evidence: the advance is compensation (ʿiwaḍ) for the wife's confinement (iḥtibās). The right to compensation ends with death, so the prepaid portion for the unexpired period is void. He analogizes to a qadi's salary or a soldier's stipend.
Al-Shafi'i agrees with this reasoning.
4
Analyze the underlying analogy (qiyas)
Muhammad's qiyas: asl is the qadi who is prepaid but dies mid-term — the unearned portion returns. Far' is the wife's advance nafaqah. 'Illah is that the payment is tied to a future service. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf reject this: they say nafaqah is a gift, not tied to future service; the sole cause (sabab) is marriage, which ended with death but possession was already complete.
The two sides differ on the nature of nafaqah: is it a countervalue for confinement or a maintenance gift?
5
Note the exception from Muhammad
Muhammad reportedly says that if the advance is for a month or less, it is considered a current payment (ḥukm al-ḥāl) and nothing is taken back. This narrows the practical difference.
This exception indicates that the dispute mainly concerns large advances.

Muhammad said: The nafaqah for the time that has passed is calculated and given to her, and the rest belongs to the husband. And this is the saying of al-Shafi'i. And on this is the difference in clothing (kiswah). Because she took in advance a compensation for what she deserves from him due to her confinement, and the entitlement became void with death, so the compensation becomes void to that extent, like the salary of a qadi and the stipend of fighters. And the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argue: It is a gift and possession has been connected to it, and there is no return in gifts after death because their ruling is complete, as in hibah. For this reason, if it perished without her consuming it, nothing is taken back from her by consensus. Explanation: If the husband gave his wife the nafaqah of a year in advance, then before that period passes, the husband dies or his...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of advance payment of nafaqah and the effect of the husband's subsequent death. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf hold that if the husband gives his wife a year's nafaqah in advance and then dies, nothing can be taken back from the wife. Their reasoning: nafaqah is a gift (ṣilah) that the wife has already taken possession of; after death, gifts cannot be reclaimed. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, aligning with Imam al-Shafi'i, disagrees. He holds that the nafaqah should be apportioned: the wife keeps only what corresponds to the time that has passed until death, and the remainder returns to the husband's estate. For Muhammad, the nafaqah is a compensation (ʻiwaḍ) for the wife's confinement (iḥtibās), and this entitlement ends with death, so the advance payment becomes void for the future period. He analogizes to a qadi's salary or a soldier's stipend, where payment is tied to service, and if service ends, the prepaid portion for the unserved period must be returned. The text also notes that the same ikhtilaf applies to clothing (kiswah) advanced. Additionally, there is a transmission from Muhammad that if the advance was small (up to a month or less), nothing is taken back even according to him, because it is considered a current payment. The passage also mentions that if the advanced nafaqah perishes without the wife's misuse, there is consensus that nothing is taken back.

Key Terms
اسلفها (asalafahā): He gave her in advance; prepayment of nafaqah before it is due.
استعجلت عوضا (istaʿjalat ʿiwaḍan): She hastened (took) a compensation in advance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The wife returns the nafaqah for the remaining six months
B) The wife keeps the entire advance and nothing is taken back
C) The wife keeps only half
D) The amount is divided equally among the heirs

Key Takeaway
Advance nafaqah is treated as a gift by the majority of the Hanafi school, so it is not recoverable after the husband's death; but there is a valid minority view requiring proportional adjustment.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
6 min
Advance Payment of Nafaqah and Death of Husband
Dispute on whether advance nafaqah is recoverable upon husband's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on recoverability of advance nafaqah upon the husband's death and evaluate the proofs for each position.

Core Idea
Advance nafaqah is irrevocable upon death — the Imamayn treat it as a gift, not compensation.

When a husband advances his wife’s annual maintenance and then dies, should the estate recover the unused portion? The Hanafi school itself differs: the Imamayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold no recovery, while Imam Muhammad argues for pro-rating. This dispute turns on the fundamental nature of nafaqah: is it a gift or compensation for confinement? In this lesson, you will analyze the arguments and understand which position is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the parties
Recognize that the dispute is about advance payment of nafaqah when the husband dies before the period ends. The Imamayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) take one view, while Imam Muhammad (and al-Shafi'i) take another.
2
State the Imamayn's position and their proof
The Imamayn: nothing is taken back. Their proof: nafaqah is a gift (silah); once delivered and accepted, it becomes like hibah (gift) and is irrevocable after death because the ruling of a gift ends with death.
Also note that if the gifted property perishes without consumption, it is still not returned by consensus.
3
State Imam Muhammad's position and his proof
Imam Muhammad: pro-rate the nafaqah based on the time lived, and the remainder goes to the husband's estate. His proof: nafaqah is compensation (iwad) for the wife's confinement (ihitbas); upon death, the basis for confinement ends, so the compensation is void proportionally, analogous to a judge's salary or a soldier's stipend.
Imam al-Shafi'i agrees with this view.
4
Analyze the analogy (qiyas) used by each side
The Imamayn analogize to hibah (gift) – once given, it cannot be taken back. Muhammad analogizes to salary (rizq) – if the service ends, the pay stops. Determine which analogy is more fitting according to the madhhab.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical effect
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Imamayn: the wife keeps the full advance payment. This is because nafaqah is not purely compensatory; it is a gift-like obligation.
However, if the amount is small (a month or less), even Muhammad agrees nothing is taken back – it is treated as immediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he paid her the nafaqah of the year, i.e. advanced it, then he died, nothing is taken back from her. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: Her nafaqah is calculated for what has passed, and the remainder is for the husband. And this is the saying of al-Shafi'i. And the same difference applies to clothing, because she advanced a compensation for what she deserved from him due to her confinement, and the entitlement became void by death, so the compensation becomes void proportionally, like the salary of a judge and the stipend of fighters. And for the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): it is a gift, and receipt has been connected to it, and there is no return in gifts after death because their ruling ends, as in hibah. For this reason, if it perished without her consuming it, nothing is taken back from it by consensus. And from Muhammad: when she receives the nafaqah of a month or less, nothing is taken back from her because it is insignificant, so it is treated as immediate nafaqah.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on advance nafaqah (maintenance) when the husband dies before the end of the period. It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between the Imamayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad, as well as the Shafi'i position. The Imamayn hold that once the wife has taken possession of the advanced nafaqah, it is a gift (silah), and after death the gift is irrevocable just like hibah. Therefore, nothing is returned even if the husband dies. Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i analogize nafaqah to a compensation (iwad) for the wife's confinement. Since the entitlement to confinement ends with death, the compensation should be pro-rated, and the excess returned. The author notes the difference applies also to clothing. The Imamayn's proof is that nafaqah is a gift, and once delivered, it is final. The fatwa is on their position. A secondary narration from Imam Muhammad states that if the amount is small (a month or less), it is treated as immediate and not returned.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance/support that a wife is entitled to from her husband for food, clothing, shelter, etc.
ملک بضع (milk al-bud'): The husband's ownership/right over the wife's sexual availability as per the marriage contract.

Key Takeaway

Nafaqah of a Free Woman Married to a Slave
Ruling on nafaqah becoming a debt when a slave marries a free woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the nafaqah of a free woman married to a slave, explain the debt's attachment to the slave's raqabah, and identify the conditions that trigger and terminate this debt.

Core Idea
A slave's nafaqah to his free wife becomes a debt attaching to his person, enforceable by sale, but drops upon his death.

This lesson unpacks a specific case in Hanafi family law: when a slave, with his master's permission, marries a free woman, her nafaqah becomes a debt on the slave. The debt attaches to his raqabah (slave-body) like trade debts, allowing the master to sell him to satisfy it. However, the debt is not a permanent liability—it drops if the slave dies or is killed. Understanding this ruling requires appreciating the interplay between the slave's legal capacity and his status as property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a slave marrying a free woman with his master's permission. The ruling is that the nafaqah becomes a debt on the slave, and he can be sold to pay it.
2
Note the condition of master's permission
The debt arises only if the marriage was with the master's consent, because the master's right is affected.
3
Understand the nature of the debt
The debt is in the slave's dhimma (liability) and attaches to his raqabah (person/property), meaning the master may be forced to sell him.
Analogous to trade debts of a merchant slave.
4
Analyze the analogy to trade debts
Just as a merchant slave's trade debts attach to his raqabah, so does this nafaqah debt, because both are obligations arising from actions permitted by the master.
This is a clear analogy (qiyas jali).
5
Explain the termination of the debt
If the slave dies or is killed, the debt drops because it is considered a gift-like obligation that does not survive the slave.
The correct view (as-sahih) holds that it drops upon killing as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And when a slave marries a free woman, her nafaqah is a debt upon him, and he is sold for it. Its meaning: when he marries with the permission of the master, because it is a debt that became obligatory in his liability due to the existence of its cause, and its obligation has become apparent with respect to the master, so it attaches to his slave-body (raqabah) like the debt of trade in the merchant slave. And the master has the option to ransom him, because her right is in the nafaqah, not in the slave-body itself. And if the slave dies, the nafaqah is dropped. Similarly, if he is killed, according to the correct view, because it is a gift.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage states the ruling when a slave marries a free woman. The nafaqah becomes a debt on the slave, and if he fails to pay, he can be sold to satisfy the debt. This applies only when the slave marries with his master's permission. The debt attaches to the slave's person (raqabah) analogously to trade debts of a merchant slave. The master may ransom the slave by paying the debt himself. The wife's right is only for the nafaqah, not for the slave himself. If the slave dies, the nafaqah is dropped because it was a gift-like obligation. Similarly, if the slave is killed, the debt does not survive according to the correct view.

Key Terms
رقبہ (raqabah): The slave's person/capital; the slave himself as property.
دين تجارة (dayn al-tijarah): Trade debts incurred by a merchant slave with permission; these also attach to the slave's raqabah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a slave's nafaqah to his free wife is a debt attached to his raqabah, enforceable by sale, but dropped upon his death or killing.

Nafaqah — Maintenance
beginner
6 min
Ruling on Maintenance of a Slave-Wife When Master Provides Separate Accommodation
Explains nafaqah obligation for a free husband of a slave-wife based on retention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a husband must provide nafaqah for his slave-wife and identify when the obligation lapses.

Core Idea
Nafaqah for a slave-wife is wajib only when the master provides separate accommodation, creating retention (ihtibas).

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses a specific marital scenario: a free man married to a slave-woman owned by another. The obligation of maintenance (nafaqah) hinges on whether the master has given the wife separate quarters. This lesson unpacks the rule, its rationale, and edge cases including voluntary service and analogous categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Scenario
Read the opening: a free man marries a slave-woman owned by someone else. Note the key actors: husband, wife (slave), master.
The wife's status as a slave affects nafaqah obligations.
2
State the Condition for Nafaqah
The master must provide separate accommodation (tabwiyah) for the couple. If he does, nafaqah becomes wajib; if not, no obligation.
Tabwiyah means the master allows her to live with her husband and does not demand her service.
3
Explain the Rationale
Nafaqah is compensation for retention (ihtibas). When the master provides accommodation, the wife is effectively retained for the husband, so he must pay.
If no accommodation, retention is absent, so no nafaqah.
4
Identify Exceptions and Analogies
Note: if the master takes the wife back into service, nafaqah lapses. But voluntary service by the wife without demand does not affect it. Also, mudabbara and umm walad are treated the same.
The master is not obligated to provide accommodation; it is his choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب النفقة تشريح اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد تایم مسئلہ ہے کہ اگر آزاد مرد نے کسی شخص کی باندی سے نکاح کیا اور مولیٰ نے اپنی اس باندی کو اس کے شوہر کے ساتھ رات میں الگ رہنے دیا تو شوہر پر اس کا نفقہ واجب ہوگا۔ کیونکہ باندی کی جانب سے احتباس پایا گیا اور نفقہ جزا احتباس ہے اور اگر مولی نے الگ ٹھکانا نہیں دیا تو شوہر پر نفقہ واجب نہیں [؟]۔ کیونکہ احتباس نہیں پایا گیا۔ اور تبویہ یعنی رات کو الگ بسانے اور ٹھکانا دینے سے مراد یہ ہے کہ مولیٰ اپنی اس باندی کو شوہر کے گھر میں رہنے دے اور باندی سے اپنی خدمت نہ لئے اور اگر مولی نے پہلے ٹھکانا دیدیا یا پھر باندی کو اپنی خدمت میں لے لیا تو نفقہ ساقط ہو جائے گا کیونکہ احتباس جو موجب نفقہ ہے وہ فوت ہو گیا۔ اس لئے شوہر پر نفقہ واجب نہیں ہوگا اور مولی پر بسانا اور ٹھکانا دینا لازم نہیں ہے جیسا کہ باب نکاح الرقیق میں گذر چکا اور اگر باندی نے خود کبھی کبھی مولی کی خدمت کی بغیر اس کے کہ مولیٰ اس سے اپنی خدمت کو کہے تو اس صورت میں نفقہ ساقط نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ مولیٰ نے اس کو واپس لینے کے طور پر خدمت نہیں لی ہے اور مدبرہ اور ام ولد اس حکم میں باندی کے مانند ہیں۔ یعنی جس طرح بسانے سے پہلے باندی کے لئے نفقہ نہیں ہوگا۔ اسی طرح مدبرہ اور ام ولد کے واسطے بھی بسانے سے پہلے نفقہ نہیں ہوگا۔
Chapter of Maintenance. Commentary: The issue is that if a free man marries someone's slave-woman, and the master allows that slave-woman to stay separately with her husband at night, then the husband's maintenance for her is obligatory. Because retention from the slave-woman's side is found, and maintenance is the compensation for retention. And if the master does not give separate accommodation, then maintenance is not obligatory upon the husband, because retention is not found. And 'tabwiyah' meaning giving separate quarters at night and providing accommodation means that the master allows that slave-woman to stay in the husband's house and does not take service from the slave-woman. And if the master first gave accommodation or then took the slave-woman into his service, then the maintenance lapses because the retention that necessitates maintenance has ceased. Therefore, maintenance is not obligatory upon the husband, and it is not necessary for the master to house and provide accommodation, as has passed in the Chapter on Marriage of Slaves. And if the slave-woman herself sometimes serves the master without the master asking her for service, then in that case maintenance does not lapse, because the master did not take service in the manner of taking her back. And a mudabbara and umm walad are like a slave-woman in this ruling. That is, just as before giving accommodation there is no maintenance for a slave-woman, similarly for a mudabbara and umm walad there is no maintenance before giving accommodation.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Chapter of Maintenance (Nafaqah) in the commentary of Al-Hidayah. It addresses a specific scenario: a free man marries a slave-woman owned by another person. The key determinant for the husband's obligation to provide nafaqah is whether the master has given the slave-wife separate accommodation (tabwiyah). If the master allows her to stay with her husband and refrains from taking her service, then the husband must pay maintenance, because the wife is effectively retained (ihtibas) for him, and nafaqah is compensation for that retention. If the master does not provide such accommodation, there is no retention, so no nafaqah. The text clarifies that 'master provides accommodation' means the master lets the slave-woman live in the husband's house and does not demand her service. If the master later takes her back into service, the nafaqah stops. The master is not obligated to provide such accommodation; it is his choice. An important nuance: if the slave-woman voluntarily serves the master without being asked, the nafaqah remains, because the master is not reclaiming her. The ruling extends to a mudabbara (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) and an umm walad (a slave who has borne her master a child); they are treated like a full slave-woman in this regard.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance, i.e., provision of food, clothing, and shelter that a husband must provide for his wife.
احتباس (ihtibas): Retention or confinement of the wife for the husband's exclusive use; the legal basis for the obligation of nafaqah.
تبویہ (tabwiyah): Giving separate accommodation or quarters by the master to the slave-wife so that she can live with her husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition is the husband not required to pay nafaqah for his slave-wife?
A) When the master provides separate accommodation
B) When the master does not provide separate accommodation
C) When the slave-wife voluntarily serves the master
D) When the slave-wife is a mudabbara

Key Takeaway
The obligation of nafaqah for a slave-wife depends on the master's provision of separate accommodation, which establishes retention (ihtibas).

Husband's Duty: Separate Accommodation for Wife
Ruling with proof: obligation of separate housing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the husband's obligation to provide separate housing, identify the Quranic proof, and explain when the wife may waive this right.

Core Idea
A wife's right to separate housing from in-laws is wajib; only she can waive it.

This passage details a critical aspect of marital maintenance (nafaqah): the husband must provide his wife with a home separate from his own family. The ruling is rooted in the Quran and ensures the wife's comfort, safety, and marital intimacy. The commentary within the source also discusses a subtle point of legal reasoning regarding the proof text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling's hukm
Determine the legal status of providing separate accommodation: is it fard, wajib, sunnah, etc.? State it clearly.
The text uses 'واجب' (wajib).
2
Locate the Quranic proof
Find the verse cited — Surah al-Talaq 65:6 — and note the specific qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud mentioned. Understand how the imperative form indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
The verse: 'اسكنوهن من حيث سكنتم وانفقوا عليهن من وجدكم'
3
Analyse the harm rationale
The text gives three harms if the wife lives with in-laws: lack of safety for belongings, inhibition in intimacy, and restriction on conjugal relations. List them.
These harms justify the separate accommodation as an exclusive right.
4
Recognise the wife's right to waive
The husband cannot force cohabitation with his family, but the wife may voluntarily choose to live with them. Note that this is a waiver of her right, not a requirement.
Waiver is valid because it is her exclusive right.
5
Examine the sharh critique
The commentator critiques Al-Hidayah's author for adding an extra justification (juxtaposition of housing and maintenance in the verse). Understand why the commentator says the imperative alone suffices.
The critique shows careful usul reasoning within the Hanafi school.

Section: And it is obligatory upon the husband to house her in a separate house in which there is none of his family, unless she chooses that. Because housing is part of her maintenance, so it is obligatory for her like maintenance. And Allah the Exalted made it obligatory coupled with maintenance. And when her right is obligatory, it is not for him to share others in it, because she is harmed by it. For she will not be safe for her belongings, and it prevents her from associating with her husband and from enjoyment. Unless she chooses, because she is satisfied with the diminution of her right. Translation: And it is obligatory upon the husband that he settle the woman in a separate house in which there is none of his family, unless the woman herself likes it. Because housing is among the wife's necessities, so it is obligatory for her like maintenance. And Allah the Exalted made it obligatory coupled with maintenance. And when housing is an obligatory right for the woman, it is not permissible for the man to share others in it, because the woman is harmed by it. For she will not be safe for her belongings, and sharing will prevent her from mixing freely with her husband, and there will be prevention from intercourse and its related matters. Unless the woman chooses, because she is satisfied with the diminution of her right. Commentary: After explaining the wife's maintenance, now the rulings of housing are stated. Thus he said: It is obligatory upon the husband that he settle his wife in a separate house in which none of the husband's family lives. Yes, if the woman herself likes to live with the husband's family, she has the option. The proof is that housing is among the wife's necessities, so it is obligatory upon the husband like maintenance. And in the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud [the verse]: 'House them where you dwell and spend on them from your wealth' (interpretation of Quran 65:6). In this verse, Allah the Exalted made housing obligatory coupled with maintenance. So it is established that housing is obligatory upon the husband like maintenance. Here the author of Hidayah said 'and He made it obligatory coupled with maintenance' – there was no need for this because juxtaposition in wording does not indicate juxtaposition in ruling. Second, to prove the obligation of housing, the saying of Allah the Exalted 'house them' was sufficient, because this is an imperative form, and the imperative form indicates obligation. In any case, when housing is an obligatory right for the woman, it is not permissible for the man to share others in it, because the woman is harmed by it – first, because she will not be safe for her belongings; second, because out of shame from others she cannot be at ease with her husband; third, because there will be prevention from intercourse and its related matters. But if the woman herself is satisfied with living with the in-laws, it is permissible for her, because she is satisfied with the diminution of her right. That is, the husband's family were prevented from living with her due to her right, but when she herself has waived her right, there is no room for objection.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage contains both the matn (Arabic) and its Urdu commentary (sharh) regarding the husband's obligation to provide separate accommodation (sukna) for his wife. The ruling states that the husband must give his wife a house separate from his own family members, unless the wife voluntarily agrees to live with them. The proof is twofold: first, housing is part of the wife's kifayah (needs), so it is wajib like nafaqah; second, the Quranic verse (65:6) in the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud couples housing with maintenance ('house them... and spend on them'). The commentator notes that the author of Al-Hidayah added an extra justification: the juxtaposition of housing and maintenance in the verse. The commentator argues that this addition is unnecessary because the imperative form 'askunuhunna' itself indicates obligation, and also because juxtaposition in wording does not necessarily imply legal correlation. Nonetheless, the obligation is clear. The husband cannot force his wife to live with his relatives because it harms her: she fears for her belongings, feels shy, and is restrained from intimacy. The wife may waive this right if she chooses.

Key Terms
سکنی (sukna): Housing or accommodation that the husband must provide for his wife as part of her maintenance.
کفایہ (kifayah): Necessities of life, including food, clothing, and housing, that the husband must provide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The verse 'House them where you dwell' in the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud
C) A hadith about the Prophet's treatment of his wives
D) The consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
The husband must provide his wife with a separate dwelling from his family; this is a wajib right that only she can waive.

Husband Cannot House His Son from Another Wife with His Current Wife
Prohibits housing stepson with wife due to harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the husband may not house his son from another wife with his current wife, and explain the reason based on harm (darar).

Core Idea
A stepson is not a mahram; cohabitation harms the wife's privacy and is prohibited.

In this portion, we see a specific application of the general principle that the wife's right to privacy (istiqlal bi'l-sakan) must be respected. The husband's son from another wife is not a mahram to the current wife, so forcing them to share accommodation would cause harm. The text emphasizes that this prohibition stands regardless of the son's age.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
What is the husband doing? He wants to house his son from another wife with his current wife.
2
State the ruling
It is not permissible (la yajuz) for him to do so.
The ruling is prohibition (haram).
3
Recall the underlying principle
The reason given is 'li-ma bayyanna' — we have explained earlier that the wife is harmed by living with non-mahram males.
The principle of harm (darar) is invoked.
4
Apply the principle
Since the stepson is not a mahram, his presence violates the wife's right to privacy and seclusion.
Age of the stepson is irrelevant.
5
Note any exceptions
The text does not provide exceptions; the prohibition is absolute.
If the wife consents voluntarily, some schools may permit, but the Hanafi view is based on harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ان كان له ولدمن غيرها فليس له ان يسكنه معها لما بينا و لو اسكنها فى بيت من الدار مفر دوله غلق كفاها لان المقصود قد حصل ترجمہ اور اگر شوہر کا لڑکا اس کے علاوہ دوسری بیوی سے ہو، تو شوہر کو اختیار نہیں کہ لڑکے کو اس کے ساتھ بسادے ۔ اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے بیان کی ہے اور اگر عورت کو شوہر نے گھر کے ایک تنہا کمرے میں جس کا بند کرنے کا دروازہ موجود ہو آباد کیا تو اس عورت کے واسطے کافی ہے۔ کیونکہ مقصود حاصل ہو گیا۔ تشریح اس عبارت میں دو مسئلے زیر بحث ہیں اول یہ کہ اگر شوہر کا لڑکا پہلی بیوی سے ہو تو شوہر کے لئے جائز نہیں کہ وہ لڑکے کو اس بیوی کے ساتھ آباد کرے۔ اس کی دلیل سابق میں گذر چکی کہ عورت کو ضرر ہوگا۔ دوسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے عورت کو گھر کے ایک تنہا کمرے میں جس کا بند کرنے کا دروازہ موجود ہے آباد کیا تو عورت کیلئے کافی ہے۔ کیونکہ مقصود حاصل ہو گیا۔ یعنی بغیر کراہت کے اس کے ساتھ جماع کرنا ممکن ہے۔
And if he has a son from another (wife), then it is not for him to house him with her, for the reason we have explained. And if he houses her in a separate room of the house that has a lock, it is sufficient for her, because the purpose has been achieved. Translation: And if the husband has a son from another wife, then the husband does not have the option to settle the son with her. For the reason we have explained. And if the husband settles the woman in a separate room of the house that has a door that can be closed, it is sufficient for her, because the purpose has been achieved. Commentary: In this phrase, two issues are discussed: First, if the husband has a son from a previous wife, it is not permissible for the husband to settle that son with this wife. Its proof has passed earlier: the woman will be harmed. Second, if the husband settles the woman in a separate room of the house that has a door that can be closed, it is sufficient for her, because the purpose has been achieved, i.e., intercourse is possible without dislike.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on housing rights. Two rulings are given. First, the husband cannot house his son from another wife with his present wife, because the same harm applies as with other family members — the wife's right to privacy and comfort is compromised. The reasoning is the same as in the previous section: the wife would be harmed by living with a non-mahram stepson. Second, if the husband provides a separate room within the same house that can be locked and is exclusively for the wife, this satisfies the requirement of separate accommodation. The key is that the wife has exclusive access to the room and can secure it, so the purpose of privacy is achieved.

Key Terms
بیٹا (beta): Here it refers to the husband's son from another wife, who is a step-relative to the current wife but not a mahram.

Separate Lockable Room Satisfies Wife's Accommodation Requirement
Determines sufficiency of a lockable separate room for wife's housing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that a separate lockable room fulfills the husband's duty to provide suitable accommodation for his wife.

Core Idea
A separate room with a lock gives the wife privacy; that meets the purpose of accommodation.

After establishing the prohibition of housing non-mahram relatives, the text now clarifies that providing a separate lockable room within the same house is sufficient for the wife's accommodation. The key is that the wife has exclusive access and can secure her privacy. This ruling balances the husband's financial limitations with the wife's right to a private space.

Guided Path
1
Identify the provision
The husband provides a room within the house for the wife.
2
State the ruling
Such a room is sufficient (kafaha) for the wife's accommodation requirement.
The room must be separate and lockable.
3
Identify the conditions
The room must be separate (mufrad) and have a lock (ghalaq).
Separation ensures privacy; lock ensures security.
4
Explain the purpose
The purpose (al-maqsud) is to enable marital intimacy without disturbance.
If the purpose is achieved, the accommodation is sufficient.
5
Consider implications
If the room does not have a lock or is shared, the wife may demand better accommodation.
The wife's right to a private space is paramount.

Source Text

و ان كان له ولدمن غيرها فليس له ان يسكنه معها لما بينا و لو اسكنها فى بيت من الدار مفر دوله غلق كفاها لان المقصود قد حصل ترجمہ اور اگر شوہر کا لڑکا اس کے علاوہ دوسری بیوی سے ہو، تو شوہر کو اختیار نہیں کہ لڑکے کو اس کے ساتھ بسادے ۔ اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے بیان کی ہے اور اگر عورت کو شوہر نے گھر کے ایک تنہا کمرے میں جس کا بند کرنے کا دروازہ موجود ہو آباد کیا تو اس عورت کے واسطے کافی ہے۔ کیونکہ مقصود حاصل ہو گیا۔ تشریح اس عبارت میں دو مسئلے زیر بحث ہیں اول یہ کہ اگر شوہر کا لڑکا پہلی بیوی سے ہو تو شوہر کے لئے جائز نہیں کہ وہ لڑکے کو اس بیوی کے ساتھ آباد کرے۔ اس کی دلیل سابق میں گذر چکی کہ عورت کو ضرر ہوگا۔ دوسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے عورت کو گھر کے ایک تنہا کمرے میں جس کا بند کرنے کا دروازہ موجود ہے آباد کیا تو عورت کیلئے کافی ہے۔ کیونکہ مقصود حاصل ہو گیا۔ یعنی بغیر کراہت کے اس کے ساتھ جماع کرنا ممکن ہے۔
And if he has a son from another (wife), then it is not for him to house him with her, for the reason we have explained. And if he houses her in a separate room of the house that has a lock, it is sufficient for her, because the purpose has been achieved. Translation: And if the husband has a son from another wife, then the husband does not have the option to settle the son with her. For the reason we have explained. And if the husband settles the woman in a separate room of the house that has a door that can be closed, it is sufficient for her, because the purpose has been achieved. Commentary: In this phrase, two issues are discussed: First, if the husband has a son from a previous wife, it is not permissible for the husband to settle that son with this wife. Its proof has passed earlier: the woman will be harmed. Second, if the husband settles the woman in a separate room of the house that has a door that can be closed, it is sufficient for her, because the purpose has been achieved, i.e., intercourse is possible without dislike.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on housing rights. Two rulings are given. First, the husband cannot house his son from another wife with his present wife, because the same harm applies as with other family members — the wife's right to privacy and comfort is compromised. The reasoning is the same as in the previous section: the wife would be harmed by living with a non-mahram stepson. Second, if the husband provides a separate room within the same house that can be locked and is exclusively for the wife, this satisfies the requirement of separate accommodation. The key is that the wife has exclusive access to the room and can secure it, so the purpose of privacy is achieved.

Key Terms
مفرد (mufrad): Separate, exclusive to the wife.
غلق (ghalaq): Lock or something that closes securely.
مقصود (maqsud): Purpose; here, the ability to have marital relations without disturbance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It has a window and is well-ventilated
B) It has a lockable door
C) It is on a separate floor
D) It is furnished by the husband

Key Takeaway
A lockable separate room fulfills the husband's duty to provide suitable accommodation for his wife.
