Taking a Surety from the Wife for Maintenance from an Absent Husband
Ruling on taking a kafil from the wife with a comparison to inheritance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking a surety (kafil) from a wife claiming maintenance from an absent husband and explain the legal distinction between this case and inheritance distribution.

Core Idea
Taking a surety protects the absent husband's right when the wife's entitlement is uncertain; inheritance requires no surety because the potential beneficiary is unknown.

This lesson examines a ruling from Al-Quduri on how the qadi handles a woman's claim for maintenance from an absent husband. The qadi takes a surety to safeguard the husband's interests, drawing a clear legal distinction from inheritance cases where heirs are present and no surety is taken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State that the qadi takes a surety (kafil) from the woman when she claims maintenance from an absent husband.
This is a recommendation (sunnah/mustahab), not an obligation.
2
Understand the Rationale
The reason is the qadi fears that the woman may have already received the maintenance before the husband's departure, or that the husband may have divorced her and her iddah ended, thereby terminating her entitlement.
The surety protects the husband's right to reclaim the amount if it turns out she was not entitled.
3
Distinguish from Inheritance
In inheritance, when heirs present evidence and do not admit other heirs, the qadi distributes the estate without taking a surety. Why? Because the potential beneficiary of any mistaken payment is unknown (the heir who might appear later). In maintenance, the husband is a known person, so a surety is possible and prudent.
This distinction is based on the certainty of the beneficiary.
4
Note the Oath
Before taking the surety, the qadi makes the woman swear by Allah that she has not received the maintenance, as an additional precaution.
The oath confirms her claim before the surety is implemented.
5
Conclude the Hukm Level
The text implies that taking a surety is recommended (sunnah/mustahab), not obligatory. The qadi may still award maintenance without a surety if he deems it appropriate.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وياخذ منها كفيلا نظر اللغائب لانهار بما استوفت النفقة او طلقها الزوج وانقضت عدتها فرق بين هذا و بين الميراث اذا قسم بين ورثة حضور بالبيئة و لم يقولوا لا نعلم له وارثا اخر حيث لا يؤخذ منهم الكفيل عنه ابي حنيفة لان هناك المكفول له مجهول وههنا معلوم و هو الزوج و يحلفها بالله ما اعطاها النفقة نظر للغائب

ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا اور قاضی اس عورت سے کفیل لے لے گا۔ تا کہ مرد غائب کی نگہداشت رہے۔ کیونکہ وہ سکتا ہے کہ اس عورت نے (اپنا) نفقہ پیشگی وصول کر لیا ہو یا شوہر اس کو طلاق دے چکا اور عدت گذر چکی ہو۔ ( امام ابوحنیفہ نے ) اس کے درمیان اور میراث کے درمیان فرق کیا ہے۔ جبکہ میراث تقسیم کی گئی موجود ورثہ کے درمیان (جن کا ثبوت ) گواہوں کے ذریعہ سے ہوا اور انہوں نے یہ نہیں کہا ہم ان کے سوا دوسرا وارث نہیں جانتے ہیں ۔ تو ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ان سے کفیل نہیں لیا جائے گا۔ اسلئے کہ وہاں ممکفول لۂ مجہول ہے اور یہاں معلوم اور وہ شوہر ہے اور قاضی اس عورت کو قسم دے گا کہ خدا کی قسم اس کو شوہر نے نفقہ نہیں دیا تا کہ مرد غائب کی نگہداشت رہے۔
He said: And he takes a surety from her, out of consideration for the absent one, because he fears that she may have already received the maintenance, or the husband may have divorced her and her waiting period ended. He distinguished between this and inheritance when it is divided among present heirs with evidence, and they do not say 'We do not know of any other heir for him' – where a surety is not taken from them according to Abu Hanifa, because there the beneficiary of the surety is unknown, while here it is known, namely the husband. And he makes her swear by Allah that he has not given her the maintenance, out of consideration for the absent one.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This is a ruling from the Quduri text, explained in the Urdu commentary. The qadi takes a surety (kafil) from a woman claiming maintenance from an absent husband to protect the husband's interests. The reason is the possibility that she may have already received the maintenance before his departure, or that he divorced her and her iddah ended, in which case she is not entitled to maintenance. Abu Hanifa distinguished this from inheritance: when heirs present evidence and do not admit any other heirs, the qadi divides the inheritance without taking a surety, because the potential beneficiary of any mistaken payment is unknown. In maintenance, the beneficiary (the husband) is known, so a surety is taken to secure his right to reclaim the amount if she was not entitled. Before taking the surety, the qadi makes the woman swear that she has not received the maintenance.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafil): A surety or guarantor who undertakes to return the maintenance amount if the woman was not entitled to it.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance that a husband owes to his wife, including food, clothing, and shelter.
غائب (gha'ib): A person who is absent from the location where the legal claim is made; here, the husband who is away.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the qadi take a surety from the wife when awarding maintenance from an absent husband?
A) To punish the wife for delaying the claim.
B) To protect the husband's right in case she already received maintenance or her entitlement ended.
C) To ensure the wife does not flee the city.
D) Because the husband is a minor.

Key Takeaway
The qadi takes a surety from a wife claiming maintenance from an absent husband to protect his right to reclaim if she was not entitled, a precaution not needed in inheritance where the beneficiary is unknown.

Maintenance from Absent Husband: The Exceptional Rule
Ruling limiting maintenance awards from absent property to specific relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the original Hanafi ruling on maintenance from absent property and explain the distinction between pre-existing wajib maintenance and ijtihadi maintenance.

Core Idea
Maintenance for wife, children, and parents is wajib before the qadi; for others, it is ijtihadi and cannot be awarded from absent property.

This passage from Al-Quduri presents a key ruling on maintenance (nafaqah) when the husband is absent. The qadi is generally prohibited from awarding maintenance from the absent person's property, except for a specific group of relatives. The distinction rests on when the obligation of maintenance becomes binding. Understanding this rule reveals how the Hanafi school navigates between protecting rights and avoiding judgment against an absent party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception
Which relatives are excepted from the general prohibition? The text lists them as the wife, minor children, and parents. Note that these are the core dependents whose maintenance is wajib regardless of a qadi's order.
The list is exhaustive for the exception; all other relatives fall under the general rule.
2
Understand the reasoning
The text explains that for the excepted relatives, maintenance is obligatory even before the qadi's ruling. Therefore, the qadi's award is merely an aid to enforce a pre-existing right. For others, maintenance is only established by the qadi's ruling, which would involve judgment against an absent person—something not permitted.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on the principle that judgment against the absent is invalid.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Zufar (Zafar) disagrees with the original position. He argues that the qadi should award maintenance for all relatives because of the woman's need and because there is no harm to the absent husband—he can defend himself when present.
Zufar's view is also within the Hanafi school, showing ijtihad within the madhhab.
4
Examine the later practice
The text states that judges today act on Zufar's opinion due to people's need. This shift from the original zahir al-riwayah position to a later fatwa illustrates how necessity (hajah) can influence legal practice.
This is an example of tarjih based on practical necessity.

He said: And he does not award maintenance from the property of an absent person except to these. And the reason for the distinction is that the maintenance of these is obligatory before the qadi's judgment, and therefore they were allowed to take it before the judgment, so the qadi's judgment is an aid to them. As for other relatives, their maintenance is only obligatory by the qadi's judgment because it is a matter of ijtihad, and judgment against an absent person is not permissible. And if the qadi does not know of this, and there is no one to acknowledge it, and she presents evidence of the marriage, or he left no property, and she presents evidence for the qadi to fix her maintenance on the absent person and order her to borrow – the qadi does not judge this because it involves judgment against an absent person. Zufar said: He judges it out of consideration for her, and there is no harm to the absent person. For if he returns and confirms her, then she has taken her right; if he denies, he is made to swear; if he refuses, she is believed; if she presents evidence, her right is proven; if she is unable, the surety or the woman guarantees. And today the practice of the judges is this: that he awards maintenance from the absent person due to the people's need, and it is a matter of ijtihad. In this issue there are other opinions that have been retracted, so we have not mentioned them.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This is a ruling from Al-Quduri that the qadi cannot award maintenance from the property of an absent person except to specific relatives: the wife, minor children, and parents. The reason is that these relatives have an obligation of maintenance established before any qadi's ruling; the qadi's role is merely to assist them in enforcing a pre-existing obligation. For other relatives (like adult siblings, uncles, etc.), maintenance is only established by a qadi's judgment because it is subject to ijtihad (disagreement among schools). Since the Hanafi school does not permit judgment against an absent person (qada' ala al-gha'ib), the qadi cannot issue such a ruling for them. The text then discusses a sub-case: if the woman's marriage is not known and the custodian of the property does not acknowledge it, she can present evidence. But the qadi cannot simply order her to borrow on the absent husband's credit, as that would be a judgment against him. Zufar (Zafar) disagreed, arguing that there is no harm to the absent husband because if he denies, he can take an oath; if he refuses, she is believed; and a surety can be taken. The practice of the later judges (qadat al-yawm) adopted Zufar's view due to people's need, as it is an ijtihadi matter. The commentary notes that earlier opinions were retracted and are not mentioned.

Key Terms
محارم (maharim): Relatives within the prohibited degrees of marriage; here referring to those entitled to maintenance like parents, children, siblings, etc.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' ala al-gha'ib): A judicial ruling against a person who is not present in court to defend themselves.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning; here, the maintenance of non-specific relatives is a matter of ijtihad, hence not settled before qadi's ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the qadi not award maintenance from an absent husband's property for relatives other than wife, minor children, and parents?
A) Because such relatives are not entitled to maintenance at all.
B) Because their maintenance is only obligatory by qadi's judgment, and judgment against an absent person is not permissible.
C) Because the absent husband is not responsible for them.
D) Because the qadi does not have the authority to order payment.

Key Takeaway
The qadi can only award maintenance from absent property to wife, minor children, and parents because their maintenance is wajib before his ruling; for others, judgment is impermissible.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Ruling on Nafaqah from an Absent Husband's Property
States the ruling and discusses Zufar's opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the original Hanafi ruling on qada' 'ala al-gha'ib regarding nafaqah and identify Zufar's position and the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A judge cannot originally order nafaqah from an absent husband, but necessity led to adopting Zufar's view.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a judge's authority to order maintenance (nafaqah) from the property of an absent husband. The original ruling prohibits it due to the principle against judgment in absentia. However, Imam Zufar argued for it based on the wife's need and protection of the husband's rights. Over time, judges adopted Zufar's view out of necessity, making it the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the original ruling
Identify the base ruling: the judge does not order the wife to borrow from the absent husband's property because it constitutes qada' 'ala al-gha'ib.
The prohibition is due to the procedural principle.
2
Present Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that the judge should order it, considering the wife's benefit and absence of harm to the husband, since the husband's rights are safeguarded through oath and evidence.
Zufar's reasoning is based on substantive justice.
3
Analyze the safeguard mechanism
Understand the procedure: if husband believes wife, she keeps; if he denies, he swears; if he refuses, she is believed; if she has evidence, it is accepted.
This balances the wife's need and the husband's rights.
4
Identify the practice of judges
Note that judges act on Zufar's view due to necessity (hajat al-nas), making it the mufta bihi.
The fatawa mutakhkhirin authority supports this shift.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: wives can now obtain maintenance from absent husbands' property through court, whereas originally they could not.
This illustrates how necessity shapes operative rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويامرها بالاستدانة لايقضى القاضي بذالك لان فی ذالك قضاء على الغائب وقال زفر يقضى فيه نظر الها ولا ضرر فيه على الغائب فانه لو حضر و صدقها فقد اخذت حقها وان جحد يحلف فان نكل فقد صدق وان اقامت بينة فقد ثبت حقهاوان عجزت يضمن الكفيل او المرأة وعمل القضاة اليوم على هذا انه يقضى بالنفقة على الغائب لحاجة الناس وهو مجتهد فيه وفى هذه المسألة اقاويل مرجوع عنها فلم ند نذكرها ولو لم يعلم القاضي بذلك
And he commands her to borrow, but the judge does not give a verdict regarding that because in that is a verdict against the absent person. Zufar said: The judge gives a verdict regarding it, looking out for her benefit, and there is no harm to the absent person. For if he were present and believed her, then she has taken her right; and if he denies, he is made to take an oath; if he refuses, she is believed; and if she produces evidence, her right is established; and if she fails, the guarantor or the woman is liable. And the practice of judges today is upon this: that the judge gives a verdict for maintenance against the absent person due to the need of the people, and it is a matter of ijtihad. And in this issue there are sayings that have been retracted, so we have not mentioned them. And if the judge does not know of that...
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding a judge's authority to order maintenance (nafaqah) from the property of an absent husband. The base ruling is that a judge cannot order the wife to borrow and then compel repayment from the absent husband's property, because that would constitute a judgment against an absent person (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib), which is not permitted in the Hanafi school. However, Imam Zufar differs: he allows the judge to order maintenance, reasoning that it benefits the wife and causes no harm to the husband, as the husband can later confirm or deny. If the husband confirms, the wife's taking is valid; if he denies, he is sworn; if he refuses the oath, she is deemed truthful; if she has evidence, it is accepted; if she fails, the guarantor or she herself is liable. The passage notes that contemporary judges (at the time of the author) act upon Zufar's view due to public necessity. This illustrates the legal principle that necessity may override the general prohibition of qada' 'ala al-gha'ib in maintenance cases. The passage also mentions that there were other opinions but they were later discarded, so they are not mentioned here. This is an example of intra-madhhab ikhtilaf where the mufta bihi (relied-upon view) became the practice of the judges.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and lodging, that a husband must provide for his wife.
غائب (gha'ib): A person who is absent from the jurisdiction and cannot defend himself in court.
كفيل (kafil): A guarantor who takes responsibility for the debt or obligation of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the original Hanafi ruling regarding a judge ordering maintenance from an absent husband's property?
A) The judge may order it immediately.
B) The judge does not order it because it constitutes a judgment against an absent person.
C) The judge orders it only if the wife presents a guarantor.
D) The judge orders it only for pregnant wives.

Key Takeaway
While the original Hanafi position prohibits ordering nafaqah from an absent husband's property due to qada' 'ala al-gha'ib, necessity led to the adoption of Zufar's view allowing it, illustrating how fiqh adapts through ijtihad.

Qadi's Ruling on Maintenance from an Absent Husband
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib for nafaqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib in maintenance cases and state the mufta bihi position adopted by later judges.

Core Idea
Qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib is impermissible, but necessity made Imam Zufar's view the operative practice.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: can a qadi order a husband to pay maintenance when he is absent? The general Hanafi rule prohibits judgment against an absentee, but Imam Zufar argues for an exception to protect the wife's welfare. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi fiqh balances principle with practical necessity, and why the operative ruling (mufta bihi) can shift over time.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi rule
Identify the principle of qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib and why it is prohibited. Note that this is the position in the Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the practical problem
Consider the wife's need for maintenance when the husband is absent and has left no property. What options are available?
3
Explain Abu Hanifa's ruling
State that Abu Hanifa holds the qadi cannot order anything because it would constitute judgment against an absentee.
4
Explain Imam Zufar's ruling and safeguards
Describe Zufar's view: the qadi can accept witnesses, take a surety (kafil) from the wife, and order maintenance from the husband's property or by borrowing. Why does he consider this permissible?
Zufar argues it benefits the wife without harming the husband, since the surety protects against false claims.
5
Note the shift in mufta bihi
Understand that al-Hidayah reports judges now follow Zufar's view due to necessity. This illustrates how a minority opinion can become operative.
The dispute shows the flexibility of the Hanafi school in responding to needs.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پس عورت نے اپنے بارے میں غائب کی بیوی ہونے پر گواہ قائم کر دیے۔ یا یہ صورت ہوئی کہ غائب نے کچھ مان نہیں چھوڑا۔ پس عورت نے اس پر گواہ قائم کیئے کہ میں اسکی بیوی ہوں تا کہ قاضی غائب پر اس کا نفقہ مقرر کر کے اس کو شوہر پر قبضہ کر لینے کا حکم دے تو قاضی ایسا حکم نہیں دے سکتا۔ کیونکہ ایسا کرنے میں قضاء علی الغائب لازم آتا ہے اور یہ ہمارے نزدیک جائز نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے قاضی اس صورت میں شوہر پر قرضہ لینے کاحکم نہیں دے سکتا۔ حضرت امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ قاضی عورت کے گواہ قبول کرے اور اس سے کفیل لے کر شوہر کے مان میں سے نفقہ دینے کا حکم دے دے اور اگر شوہر غائب کے واسطے مال نہ ہو تو شوہر پر قرضہ لینے کا حکم کرے۔ کیونکہ اس میں عورت کے واسطے بہتری ہے اور مرد غائب کے حق میں کچھ ضرر نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے کہ جب وہ حاضر ہوا اور عورت کے قول کی تصدیق کی تو ثابت ہو گیا کہ اس نے اپنا حق لیا ہے اور اگر شوہر نے عورت کے قول کی تصدیق نہیں کی بلکہ انکار کیا تو شوہر سے قسم لی جائے گی ۔ پس اگر وہ قسم سے انکار کرے تو بھی عورت کی تصدیق لا زم آئی ، اور اگر عورت نے گواہی قائم کر دیئے تو بھی عورت کا حق ثابت ہو گیا اور اگر عورت گواہ قائم کرنے سے عاجز ہوگئی اور شوہر نے قسم کہاں کہ یہ میری بیوی نہیں یا میں اس کو نفقہ دے چکا تو اس صورت میں نفقہ کی وہ مقدار جو قاضی نے شوہر کی غیبت کے زمانے میں اس کے ، اما سے دلوائی ہے اس کا ضامن اس عورت کا کفیل ہوگا۔ یا خود ہی عورت ضامن ہوگی ۔ صاحب ہدا یہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس زمانے میں قاضیوں کا عمل امام زفر کے قول پر ہے کہ قاضی مرد غائب پر نفقہ کا حکم دیتا ہے۔ کیونکہ اوول میں اس کی ضرورت ہے اور یہ مسئلہ بھی مختلف فیہ ہے اور اس مسئلہ میں دیگر اقوال بھی ہیں۔ جن سے مجتہد وں نے رجوع کیا ہے۔
So if the woman establishes witnesses that she is the wife of the absent man, or if the absent man did not leave any property, and she establishes witnesses that she is his wife so that the judge may fix her maintenance upon the absent husband and order him to hand it over, the judge cannot give such an order. Because doing so would entail qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib, and this is not permissible according to us. Therefore, the judge cannot order the husband to borrow money. Imam Abu Zufar said that the judge should accept the woman's witnesses, take a surety from her, and order maintenance to be given from the husband's property; and if the absent husband has no property, he should order him to borrow. Because this is better for the woman and does no harm to the absent man. For when he appears and confirms the woman's statement, it becomes established that she has taken her right; and if the husband does not confirm but denies, the husband will be asked to take an oath. If he refuses the oath, the woman's statement becomes binding; and if the woman establishes witnesses, her right is also established. If the woman is unable to produce witnesses and the husband swears that she is not his wife or that he has already paid maintenance, then the amount of maintenance that the judge ordered to be paid during the husband's absence is guaranteed by the woman's surety, or the woman herself becomes the guarantor. The author of al-Hidayah says that in this time, the practice of judges is according to Imam Zufar's statement: the judge orders maintenance from the absent husband. Because there is a need for it, and this issue is disputed, and there are other opinions from which the mujtahids have retracted.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on a qadi's ability to order maintenance (nafaqah) for a wife from an absent husband. The passage first states the general Hanafi position: the qadi cannot order payment from an absent husband because that would entail 'qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib' (judgment against an absentee), which is impermissible. However, Imam Zufar, a prominent Hanafi jurist, disagrees. He argues that the qadi can accept the wife's witnesses, take a surety (kafil) from her, and then order maintenance from the husband's property if available, or order the husband to borrow money if not. His reasoning is that this serves the woman's benefit without harming the absent husband, because when he returns, he can either confirm the debt or deny it, and the surety protects against loss. The passage then notes that the author of al-Hidayah (the original matn) states that in the contemporary period, judges actually act on Imam Zufar's view due to necessity and because the issue is disputed among scholars. This lesson illustrates an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (dispute) where the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) shifted over time due to practical need.

Key Terms
قضاء علی الغائب (qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib): Judgment against an absent person, generally impermissible in Hanafi law.
کفیل (kafīl): Surety; a person who guarantees the payment if the absent husband fails to confirm.
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance, including food, clothing, and shelter, owed by a husband to his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Zufar, what must the qadi take from the wife before ordering maintenance from the absent husband?
A) A written statement
B) A surety (kafil)
C) Two male witnesses
D) An oath

Key Takeaway
Although qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib is prohibited, the Hanafi school permits an exception for nafaqah with a surety, making Zufar's view the operative ruling due to necessity.

Nafaqah — Maintenance

Maintenance for the Irrevocably Divorced Woman: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on nafaqah for bā'in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof for requiring maintenance for a bā'in divorcee and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays.

Core Idea
Maintenance is compensation for retention; 'iddah retains the wife for the husband's interest in the child.

The right of a divorced woman to maintenance during her waiting period is a key point of disagreement between the Hanafi school and the Shafi'i school. This passage from al-Hidayah presents the dispute and the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i reliance on the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays. We will dissect the arguments and understand the Hanafi principle of jaza' al-ih'tibās (compensation for retention).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling and both positions
State the Hanafi position: all divorced women (raj'i or bā'in) are entitled to nafaqah and suknā during 'iddah. State the Shafi'i position: bā'in divorcees do not receive nafaqah unless pregnant.
The dispute is specific to bā'in divorce; raj'i divorce is agreed upon.
2
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Imam al-Shafi'i relies on the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays, where the Prophet did not give her maintenance or housing after three divorces. He also uses analogy to a widow, where no maintenance is due because marital ownership is severed.
Note that the hadith is a single (āḥād) narration, and the analogy to widow assumes that 'iddah is not for the child.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is that maintenance is compensation for retention (jaza' al-ih'tibās). The 'iddah is obligatory to protect the lineage (by determining pregnancy), which is a purpose of marriage. Therefore, the husband retains the wife for this purpose and must provide maintenance.
This principle is derived from the ruling for pregnant women (Qur'an 65:6) and generalized by qiyas.
4
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal and tarjih
The Hanafis reject the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays because it was contradicted by the practice of major Companions like 'Umar, who reported that the Prophet did prescribe nafaqah and suknā for a triple-divorced woman. The analogy to a widow fails because a widow's 'iddah is for mourning, not for the child.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and based on stronger evidence (rejection of the hadith and sound qiyas).

عورت حاملہ ہو تو اس کیلئے نفقہ واجب ہو گا۔ کیونکہ حاملہ عورت کے واسطے نفقہ کا واجب ہونا ہم کو بنص قرآنی معلوم ہوا۔ لعين و ان كن اولات الحمل فانفقوا عليهن ۔ اگر یہ عورتیں حمل والیاں ہوں تو ان کو نفقہ دو۔
When a man divorces his wife, whether it is a raj'i or bā'in divorce, she is entitled to maintenance and housing during her 'iddah. Imam al-Shafi'i said: There is no maintenance for the woman who is irrevocably divorced (al-mabtūtah) unless she is pregnant. As for the raj'i divorce, it is because the marriage continues after it, especially according to us, because intercourse is permissible. As for the bā'in divorce, his proof is the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays, who said: 'My husband divorced me three times, and the Messenger of Allah did not prescribe for me maintenance or housing.' Also because he (the husband) does not possess her, and maintenance is based on ownership, which is why it is not obligatory for a woman whose husband has died, due to the absence of ownership. This is unlike the case when she is pregnant, because we know from the text of the Qur'an: 'And if they are pregnant, then spend on them' (65:6). Our proof is that maintenance is compensation for retention (jaza' al-ih'tibās), as we have mentioned, and retention exists for a purpose of marriage, which is the child. Because 'iddah is obligatory for the protection of the child, so maintenance becomes obligatory. That is why housing is obligatory by consensus. So it is as if she were pregnant. The hadith of Fatimah bint Qays was rejected by 'Umar, who said: 'We will not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet for the statement of a woman, we do not know if she is truthful or lying, whether she remembered or forgot. I heard the Messenger of Allah saying: For a woman divorced three times, maintenance and housing are obligatory as long as she is in her 'iddah.' It was also rejected by Zayd ibn Thabit, Usamah ibn Zayd, Jabir, and 'Aishah. Translation: And when a man divorces his wife, maintenance and housing are obligatory for her during her 'iddah, whether the divorce is raj'i or bā'in. Imam al-Shafi'i said that for a woman who has been permanently separated (al-matūtah), there is no maintenance unless she is pregnant. In any case, for raj'i, because after a raj'i divorce the marriage remains, especially according to us, because during the 'iddah of raj'i, intercourse is permissible. As for bā'in, the basis of Imam al-Shafi'i's statement is the hadith narrated from Fatimah bint Qays, that she said: 'My husband divorced me three times, and the Messenger of Allah did not prescribe for me any maintenance or housing.' And also because the husband does not have ownership over the bā'in woman, and maintenance is based on marital ownership. For that reason, maintenance is not obligatory for a woman whose husband has died, because ownership is removed. As opposed to when she is pregnant, because the obligation of maintenance for a pregnant woman is known from the explicit Qur'anic text. And our proof is that maintenance is compensation for retention, as we have mentioned, and retention continues for a purpose of marriage, which is the child. Because 'iddah is obligatory for the protection of the child, so maintenance becomes obligatory. For that reason, housing is obligatory by consensus. So it is like the case where she is pregnant. The hadith of Fatimah bint Qays was rejected by 'Umar, who said: 'We will not abandon the Book of our Lord and the Sunnah of our Prophet for the word of a woman; we do not know if she was truthful or lying, she remembered or forgot. And I heard the Messenger of Allah saying that a woman divorced three times is entitled to maintenance and housing as long as she is in her 'iddah.' This hadith was also rejected by Zayd ibn Thabit, Usamah ibn Zayd, Jabir, and 'Aishah. Commentary of the author: Having completed the discussion of maintenance and housing during marriage, he now discusses the rulings of maintenance and housing after separation. So the scenario is: if a man divorces his wife, whether raj'i or bā'in, maintenance and housing are obligatory during her 'iddah. Imam al-Shafi'i said: for a woman who is permanently separated, such as by one bā'in divorce, three divorces, or a khul', there is no maintenance. Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad also hold this view. But if she is pregnant, there is consensus that maintenance is obligatory. In any case, for a raj'i divorce, maintenance and housing are obligatory by consensus until the end of the 'iddah. The proof is that after a raj'i divorce, the marriage continues, especially according to us because intercourse is permissible during the 'iddah of a raj'i divorce. As for bā'in, there is disagreement. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays, narrated by a group including Imam al-Bukhari. Fatimah said: 'My husband divorced me three times, and the Messenger of Allah did not prescribe for me any maintenance or housing.' His second proof is that the husband does not have ownership over a bā'in divorcee, and according to Imam al-Shafi'i, maintenance is compensation for marital ownership. Therefore, when there is no ownership, there is no maintenance. Similarly, a widow does not receive maintenance. However, if she is pregnant, maintenance is obligatory, as the Qur'an says: 'And if they are pregnant, then spend on them.' (65:6).
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents a major inter-madhhab dispute regarding maintenance (nafaqah) and housing (suknā) for divorced women during their 'iddah (waiting period). The Hanafi position, stated in the matn, is that all divorced women, whether by raj'i (revocable) or bā'in (irrevocable) divorce, are entitled to maintenance and housing during the 'iddah. Imam al-Shafi'i, along with Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad, holds that a woman who is irrevocably divorced (al-mabtūtah) is not entitled to maintenance unless she is pregnant. The passage then details the proofs for each school. The Shafi'i proof is twofold: (1) the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays, who stated that after three divorces, the Prophet ﷺ did not give her maintenance or housing; (2) analogy to a widow, who does not receive maintenance because marital ownership is severed. The Hanafi proof is that maintenance is compensation for retention (jaza' al-ih'tibās) of the woman, and retention continues because the 'iddah serves to protect the lineage (by determining if she is pregnant). Since the 'iddah is entirely for the purpose of safeguarding the child, the husband must provide maintenance as long as he is retaining her for that purpose. This also explains why housing is unanimously obligatory: it is part of the retention. The Hanafis also rebut the hadith of Fatimah bint Qays by noting that it was rejected by major Companions including 'Umar, Zayd ibn Thabit, Usamah ibn Zayd, Jabir, and 'Aishah, who reported that the Prophet ﷺ actually said that a woman divorced three times is entitled to maintenance and housing during her 'iddah. The passage thus demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to the principle that the 'iddah creates a continued relationship of entitlement, not merely a period of waiting, and this is reinforced by the rejection of a hadith that contradicted stronger evidence. The relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is that maintenance is obligatory for all divorced women during 'iddah, whether pregnant or not, though the obligation is stronger when pregnant due to explicit Qur'anic text.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance; financial support including food, clothing, and other necessities.
سکنی (suknā): Housing; the right to live in a residence during the 'iddah.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death, during which a woman cannot remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof for requiring maintenance for a bā'in divorcee during 'iddah?
A) The hadith of Fatimah bint Qays
B) Maintenance is compensation for retention, and 'iddah is for the protection of the child
C) Analogy to a widow
D) The Qur'anic verse about pregnant women only

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires maintenance and housing for all divorced women during 'iddah, based on the principle that the 'iddah retains the woman for the husband's interest in the child, making maintenance a form of compensation.

Module 10 · Lesson 37

Nafaqah — Maintenance
intermediate
6 min
Nafaqah during Iddah: When Separation is Due to the Wife's Sin
States the rule of nafaqah for wife causing separation through sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a woman in iddah loses her right to nafaqah due to her own sin, and distinguish that from cases where she retains it.

Core Idea
A wife who causes separation through sin loses nafaqah; if her action is legitimate, she keeps it.

This lesson examines a key exception to the general rule that a wife in iddah is entitled to nafaqah. The Hanafi school holds that if the separation occurs because of a sin committed by the wife—such as apostasy or an act of gross disobedience—she forfeits her maintenance. However, if her action is based on a legitimate right (like exercising an option), her nafaqah remains. We will trace the legal reasoning behind this distinction and explore practical applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of furqah
Separate separations (furqah) into those caused by the wife's sin (ma'siyah) and those caused by her without sin (bi-ghayr ma'siyah).
The ruling depends on which category the case falls into.
2
Understand the analogy to nashizah
When the wife causes separation through sin, she is like a nashizah (rebellious wife) who wrongfully withholds herself. Just as a nashizah loses nafaqah, so does the sinful wife.
The illa (effective cause) is wrongful withholding (habs bi-ghayri haqq).
3
Apply the contrast to specific examples
Work through the examples given: apostasy and kissing the husband's son with desire (sinful, no nafaqah); options of puberty/slavery and incompatibility (non-sinful, nafaqah remains).
Note that the type of separation matters, not the fact of separation itself.
4
Consider the exception regarding imprisonment
If the apostate wife is imprisoned, she loses nafaqah because imprisonment prevents her from being available. If she is not imprisoned and remains in the house, nafaqah is due.
This is from Al-Mabsut and refines the general rule.

ہے۔ پس یہ ایسی ہوگئی جیسے وہ نافرمانی کر کے گھر سے نکل گئی ہو۔ مصنف نے عبارت میں نفقہ کا ذکر کیا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ سکنی اس معتدہ کے واسطے بھی واجب ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ معدہ کیلئے گھر میں متھہرے رہنا واجب ہے۔ لہذا عورت کی معصیت سے سکنی ساقط نہیں ہوگا۔ مبسوط میں لکھا ہے کہ مردہ کا نفقہ مین ردت کی وجہ سے ساقط نہیں ہوا۔ بلکہ اسلئے ساقط ہو گا کہ ردت کی وجہ سے وہ قید اور محبوس کر لی جائے گی اور محبوسہ بحق ( جو اپنے حق میں گرفتار کر لی گئی ہو ) بحالت قیام نکاح نفقہ کی مستحق نہیں ہوتی۔ پس ایسے ہی عدت میں بھی نفقہ کی مستحق نہیں ہوگی ۔ حتی کہ اگر عورت مرتدہ ہوگئی اور محبوس نہیں کی گئی بلکہ شوہر کے گھر میں ہے تو اس کے واسطے نفقہ واجب ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ جبس اور قید کرنا نہیں پایا گیا ۔ ( مینی ، عنایہ ) اور برخلاف اس کے کہ اگر عورت وطی کے بعد متردہ ہوئی تو اس کا مہر ساقط نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مہر ملک بضع کا عوض ہوتا ہے اور عورت کی جانب سے ولی کے ساتھ تسلیم بضع پایا گیا۔ سلئے شوہر کے ذمہ سے مہر ساقط نہیں ہوگا اور اس کے برخلاف اگر فرقت عورت کی جانب سے بغیر معصیت کے پیدا ہوئی ۔ مثلاً خیار محتق اور خیار بلوغ کی وجہ سے عورت نے تفریق کی ہے۔ یا کفوء نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے تفریق کی گئی تو عدت کے زمانے میں اس عورت کے واسطے نفقہ واجب ہو گا۔ اسی طرح جس عورت نے لعان کیا اور لعان کی وجہ سے تفریق ہوئی تو اس کے واسطے بھی نفقہ اور سکنی واجب ہو گا اور اسی طرح خلع یا ایلاء کی وجہ سے بائنہ ہوگئی تو بھی اس کیلئے بھی نفقہ اور سکنی ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ ان صورتوں میں عورت نے اپنے نفس کو حق کے ساتھ روکا ہے اور ایسا رو کنا نفقہ ساقط نہیں کرتا ہے۔ جیسے اگر اس نے اپنے آپ کو شوہر کے پاس جانے سے اس واسطے روکا کہ اپنا مہر معجل وصول کرے تو نفقہ قائم رہتا ہے۔ ساقط نہیں ہوتا۔
Every separation that comes from the wife's side due to sin, such as apostasy or kissing the husband's son, has no nafaqah for her because she has made herself unlawful without right, so she becomes like one who is nashizah. Contrary to the mahr after consummation, because delivery (taslim) in the matter of mahr was achieved by intercourse. And contrary to when the separation comes from her side without sin, like the option of slavery, option of puberty, and separation due to lack of compatibility, because she detained herself rightfully, and that does not nullify nafaqah, just as if she detained herself for the collection of mahr.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with its proof and exceptions. It explains that if a woman causes separation from her husband through a sin, such as apostasy (riddah) or kissing the husband's son with desire (shahwah), she is not entitled to nafaqah (maintenance) during the iddah. The reason is that she has wrongfully prevented herself from fulfilling her marital duties, making her analogous to a nashizah (rebellious wife) who also loses the right to nafaqah. The text then makes an important contrast: if the separation comes from the wife's side without sin—for example, via the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh), option of slavery (khiyar al-‘itq), or separation due to lack of compatibility (adam al-kafa'ah)—then nafaqah remains obligatory because her action is right based on her right. Similarly, the mahr after consummation is not forfeited even if she apostasizes, because delivery of the object of mahr (i.e., the marital intimacy) has already taken place. The commentary elaborates that the sakani (housing) is still obligatory during iddah even if the wife sinned, because housing is a separate requirement. A point from Al-Mabsut clarifies that if the apostate wife is not imprisoned (mahbusah) but remains in the husband's house, nafaqah must be paid because the impediment (imprisonment) is absent. In contrast, if she is imprisoned for apostasy, nafaqah is not due. The ruling is fully explained with all its conditions.

Key Terms
فرقة (furqah): Separation or dissolution of marriage, specifically in the context of iddah.
ناشزة (nashizah): A wife who rebels against her husband's legitimate authority, e.g., leaving the marital home without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman apostatizes (murtaddah) after her husband divorces her. According to Hanafi fiqh in this passage, under what condition would she still receive nafaqah during iddah?
A) If she is imprisoned for apostasy.
B) If she is not imprisoned but remains in the husband's house.
C) If she repents before the iddah ends.
D) If the divorce is a single revocable talaq.

Key Takeaway
A wife's sinful act causing separation deprives her of nafaqah in iddah, but a legitimate action preserves it.

Nafaqah After Triple Talaq: Apostasy vs. Zina with Step-Son
Distinguishes nafaqah after triple talaq when wife apostatizes versus commits zina with step-son

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why apostasy after triple talaq forfeits nafaqah while zina with a step-son does not, and identify the critical factor of imprisonment.

Core Idea
Apostasy forfeits nafaqah due to imprisonment, not the sin; zina does not lead to automatic imprisonment, so nafaqah remains.

This passage presents two scenarios after a husband pronounces three irrevocable divorces (talaq bain). In one, the wife apostatizes; in the other, she commits zina with her step-son. Both cases involve a complete separation, yet the nafaqah rulings differ. The key lies not in the act itself but in its legal consequence: imprisonment of the apostate makes her ineligible for nafaqah, while the adulteress remains free and thus entitled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Scenario 1: Wife apostatizes after triple talaq. Scenario 2: Wife commits zina with step-son after triple talaq.
Both occur after the irrevocable divorce.
2
Note the common element
In both cases, the separation (firqah) is established by the three divorces. Apostasy and zina do not alter the fact of separation.
The cause of separation is the same, so differences in nafaqah must come from elsewhere.
3
Identify the different outcome
In scenario 1, nafaqah is forfeited. In scenario 2, nafaqah is retained.
The literal ruling is given for each.
4
Identify the underlying cause
The apostate woman is imprisoned until she repents, and a prisoner cannot fulfill the iddah residence requirement, so no nafaqah. The adulteress is not imprisoned (for that act alone), so she can fulfill iddah and receives nafaqah.
Imprisonment is the decisive factor, not the sinfulness of the act.

Source Text

و ان طلقها ثلثا ثم ارتدت والعياذ بالله سقطت نققتها وان مكنت ابن زوجها من نفسها فلها النفقة معناه مكنت بعد الطلاق لان الفرقة تثبت بالطلقات الثلث ولاعمل فيها للردة والتمكين الا ان المرتدة تحبس حتى تتوب ولانفقة للمحبوسة والممكنة لا تحبس فلهذا يقع الفرق ترجمہ اور اگر شوہر نے (اپنی بیوی کو ) تین طلاقیں دے دیں۔ اس کے بعد وہ العیاذ باللہ مرتد ہ ہوئی۔ تو اس کا نفقہ ساقط ہو گیا اور اگر اس نے اپنے شوہر کے بیٹے کو اپنے نفس پر قدرت دی۔ تو اس کیلئے نفقہ ہوگا۔ اس کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ طلاق کے بعد اس نے قدرت دی (ولی کرائی) کیونکہ فرقت تین طلاقوں کی وجہ سے ثابت ہوئی اور فرقت میں مرتد ہونے اور قدرت دینے کو کوئی دخل نہیں ہے۔ مگر یہ کہ مرتدہ محبوس کی جاتی ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ تو بہ کرے اور ایسی قیدی عورت کیلئے نفقہ نہیں ہوتا ہے اور (ابن زوج) کو قدرت دینے والی عورت قید نہ ہوگی ۔ پس اس وجہ سے ( دونوں میں ) فرق واقع ہو جائے گا۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کو تین طلاقیں دے دیں۔ اس کے بعد عورت العیاذ باللہ مرتد ہوگئی۔ تو اس عورت کا نفقہ ساقط ہو گیا اور اگر تین طلاقوں کے بعد عورت نے اپنے شوہر کے بیٹے کو ( جو پہلی بیوی سے ہے ) اپنے نفس پر قدرت دے دی۔ یعنی اس کے ساتھ اپنا منہ کالا کیا تو اس کے واسطے نفقہ واجب ہوگا۔ ان دونوں مسئلوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ دونوں صورتوں میں فرقت تین طلاقوں سے ثابت ہوئی ہے اور مرتد ہونے اور ابن زوج سے وطی کرانے کو اس فرقت میں کوئی دخل نہیں ہے۔ لیکن اتنی بات ہے کہ جو عورت مرتدہ ہوگئی وہ قید کی جاتی ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ تو بہ کرے اور ایسی قیدی عورت کے واسطے نفقہ نہیں ہوتا ہے اور جس عورت نے شوہر کے برخوردار سے ولی کرائی تو وہ قید نہیں کی جاتی ۔ اسلئے اس کے واسطے نفقہ ہوگا۔ پس اس وجہ سے دونوں مسئلوں میں فرق ہو گیا۔
And if he (the husband) gives her three divorces, then she becomes apostate (may Allah protect us), her nafaqah is forfeited. And if she gives access to her husband's son (step-son) to herself, then she has nafaqah. Meaning: she gave access after the divorce, because the separation is established by the three divorces, and apostasy or giving access has no effect on it. However, the apostate woman is imprisoned until she repents, and there is no nafaqah for the imprisoned woman, while the woman who gave access is not imprisoned; thus the difference arises.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a specific application of the general rule discussed previously. It presents two scenarios after a husband pronounces three irrevocable divorces (talaq bain). In the first scenario, the wife apostatizes after the triple talaq. The ruling is that her nafaqah is forfeited. In the second scenario, the wife commits zina with her step-son (her husband's son from another wife). Surprisingly, she retains her right to nafaqah. The key factor is external to the cause of separation: both separations are complete and unaffected by these post-talaq acts. However, the apostate woman is subject to imprisonment by the Islamic state until she repents, and a prisoner is not entitled to nafaqah because she is not available to fulfill the iddah residence requirement. The woman who commits zina is not imprisoned for that act per se (unless there is a legal prosecution, but the text assumes she is not), so she continues to fulfill the iddah residence and thus deserves nafaqah. The commentary emphasizes that the separation itself (by three talaqs) is the same in both cases; the difference arises from the state of the woman (imprisoned vs. free). This illustrates the detailed casuistry of Hanafi fiqh in applying general principles to specific situations.

Key Terms
ردة (riddah): Apostasy from Islam, which in this context triggers imprisonment until repentance.
تمكين (tamkin): Giving access (to sexual intercourse) voluntarily, here referring to the wife's zina with step-son.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a wife who commits zina with her step-son after three divorces still receive nafaqah, while a wife who apostatizes after three divorces does not?
A) Because zina is a lesser sin than apostasy.
B) Because the apostate is imprisoned and thus not eligible for nafaqah, while the adulteress is not imprisoned.
C) Because the separation after zina is not complete.
D) Because the step-son inherits the nafaqah obligation.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah after triple talaq depends on the wife's ability to fulfill iddah residence; imprisonment forfeits it, while other sins do not automatically cancel it.

Nafaqah of Minor Children is Solely on Father
Establishes the father's exclusive obligation for minor children's maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah of minor children, identify its Quranic proof, and explain why the obligation is exclusive to the father.

Core Idea
Nafaqah of minor children is wajib on the father alone — no one shares this obligation.

This lesson examines a foundational ruling in Hanafi family law: the maintenance of minor children. The source passage from Al-Hidayah establishes that this obligation falls exclusively on the father, using a Quranic verse as proof. We will explore the ruling, its evidence, and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the legal act under discussion? The nafaqah (maintenance: food, clothing, shelter) of minor children. What is the ruling? It is wajib (obligatory).
The text uses 'nafaqah' without specifying its components; they are understood from the context.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The Quranic verse 2:233: 'And on the one to whom the child is born is their provision.'
The verse continues but only the key phrase is quoted.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this verse prove the ruling? The phrase 'al-mawlud lahu' is interpreted as the biological father. The command 'rizquhunna' (their provision) indicates an obligation (wujub) according to Hanafi usul (al-amr yufidu al-wujub).
The Hanafi school holds that the general imperative indicates obligation unless specified otherwise.
4
Note the exclusivity
What does the text say about sharing? 'No one shares with him in it.' This explicitly makes the father's obligation exclusive — the mother, grandfather, or any relative is not obligated.
This exclusivity is analogous to the wife's nafaqah, which is also the husband's sole duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل. ونفقة الاولاد الصغار على الاب لايشاركه فيها احد كما لايشاركه فى نفقة الزوجة لقوله تعالى وعلى المولودله رزقهن والمولود له هو الاب ترجمہ فصل ! اور نابالغ اولاد کا نفقہ باپ پر واجب ہو گا۔ اس میں باپ کے ساتھ کوئی شریک نہ ہو گا۔ جیسے بیوی کے نفقہ میں کوئی شریک نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ کیونکہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا وعلی المولود لۂ رزقھن اور مولودلۂ باپ ہے۔ تشریح بیوی کے نفقہ سے فراغت کے بعد اس فصل میں اولاد کے نفقہ کو بیان کریں گے۔ چنانچہ فرمایا کہ نا بالغ اولا د کا نفقہ صرف ان کے باپ پر واجب ہوگا۔ اس میں باپ کے ساتھ کوئی شریک نہ ہوگا۔ جیسے اس کی بیوی کے نفقہ میں کوئی شریک نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ یہ حکم تھانہ
Chapter: And the nafaqah of minor children is on the father; no one shares with him in it, just as no one shares with him in the nafaqah of the wife. Due to the saying of Allah Almighty: 'And on the one to whom the child is born (the father) their provision is due.' And 'the one to whom the child is born' is the father.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter (fasl) on the nafaqah of children. It states a clear rule: the obligation to provide maintenance (food, clothing, shelter) for minor (saghir) children falls exclusively on their father. No one else — mother, grandfather, uncle, etc. — is obligated to share this burden with him. The analogy is given with the wife's nafaqah, which is also solely on the husband. The proof is the Quranic verse from Surat al-Baqarah (2:233): 'And on the one to whom the child is born (i.e., the father) is their provision and clothing.' The verse is interpreted to mean that the father is uniquely responsible for the children's maintenance. The commentary elaborates that this is after the section on wife's nafaqah, and it establishes the father's exclusive liability. This is a foundational ruling in Hanafi family law.

Key Terms
نفقة الأولاد الصغار (nafaqatu al-awlad al-sighar): Maintenance (food, clothing, shelter) for minor children who have not reached puberty.
المولود له (al-mawlud lahu): The person to whom the child is born, i.e., the father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mother, if she has wealth.
B) The father, with no partner sharing.
C) The paternal grandfather, after the father.
D) Both parents equally.

Key Takeaway
The nafaqah of minor children is exclusively the father's wajib duty, supported by Quran 2:233.

Nafaqah After Triple Divorce: Apostasy vs Tamkin
Contrasting nafaqah rulings for apostasy and tamkin after triple divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah after triple divorce for apostasy and tamkin, and explain the reasoning based on imprisonment.

Core Idea
Nafaqah after triple divorce drops only if the woman is imprisoned, not for the act itself.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced Hanafi ruling that distinguishes between two post-divorce scenarios: apostasy and enabling the husband's son. Both involve actions that might seem to forfeit nafaqah, but the school applies a precise legal reasoning based on the woman's imprisonment status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Read the passage and note the two scenarios: the woman apostatizes, and the woman enables the husband's son. What is the ruling for each?
2
State the ruling for each
The passage states: for apostasy, nafaqah drops; for tamkin, nafaqah remains. Write these down.
3
Identify the common principle
The passage explains that separation is already established by triple divorce, so neither act affects the marital status. What does this imply?
The acts do not change the fact that the marriage is over.
4
Identify the distinguishing factor
The passage highlights that the apostate is imprisoned until she repents, while the woman who enables the son is not imprisoned. Why is imprisonment the key?
Because nafaqah is compensation for availability; an imprisoned woman cannot be available.
5
Derive the rationale
Conclude: The ruling is based on the practical consequence (imprisonment), not on the sinfulness of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ان طلقها ثلثا ثم ارتدت والعياذ بالله سقطت نفقتها وان مكنت ابن زوجها من نفسها فلها النفقة معناه مكنت بعد الطلاق لان الفرقة تثبت بالطلقات الثلث ولاعمل فيها للردة والتمكين الا ان المرتدة تحبس حتى تتوب ولا نفقة للمحبوسة والممكنة لا تحبس فلهذا يقع الفرق
And if he divorces her three times then she apostatizes (Allah forbid), her nafaqah drops. And if she enables her husband's son to have access to herself, then she has nafaqah. Its meaning: she enabled after the divorce because the separation is established by the three divorces, and apostasy or enabling have no effect in it. However, the apostate is imprisoned until she repents, and there is no nafaqah for the imprisoned, while the one who enables is not imprisoned, so the difference occurs.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from the Hanafi school. It presents two scenarios after a triple divorce: (1) If the woman apostatizes, her nafaqah (maintenance) is dropped. (2) If she enables her husband's son (from another wife) to have intercourse with her, she is entitled to nafaqah. The reasoning is that the separation (farq) is already established by the three divorces, so neither apostasy nor tamkin (enabling) affects the marital status. The distinction arises from the practical consequence: an apostate woman is imprisoned by the authorities until she repents, and an imprisoned woman does not receive nafaqah because maintenance is for those who are free to serve their husband. The woman who enables the son is not imprisoned, so she remains eligible for nafaqah. This is a clear example of how Hanafi fiqh uses rational reasoning (ta'lil) to resolve seemingly contradictory cases.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance or financial support owed by the husband to the wife during marriage and iddah.
ارتداد (irtidad): Apostasy from Islam by a Muslim, which severs marital ties in this context.
تمكين (tamkin): Enabling a person to have sexual access; here, the divorced woman enabling the husband's son.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After a valid triple divorce, why does a woman who commits apostasy lose her nafaqah while a woman who enables her husband's son does not?
A) Because apostasy is a greater sin than zina.
B) Because the apostate is imprisoned and cannot serve the husband, while the other is free.
C) Because the marriage is invalidated by apostasy but not by zina.
D) Because the husband may forgive the zina but not apostasy.

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah after triple divorce is dropped only when the woman is imprisoned, regardless of the act that led to imprisonment.

The Father's Sole Obligation for Minor Children's Nafaqah
Establishing the father's sole obligation for minor children's nafaqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nafaqah of minor children and identify the Quranic proof and the qiyas reasoning used.

Core Idea
The father bears sole responsibility for minor children's nafaqah, with no partner sharing, based on Quran and analogy.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi family law: the obligation of a father to financially support his minor children. The passage establishes this duty as exclusive to the father, drawing on both a direct Quranic verse and an analogy from the wife's maintenance. We will unpack the proof and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the passage and extract the core legal ruling: who is responsible for the nafaqah of minor children, and does anyone share?
2
Locate the Quranic Proof
Find the verse cited (al-Baqarah 2:233) and explain how the phrase 'mawludu lahu' points to the father. Understand that the verse is about nursing mothers but the principle of provision is extended by analogy.
The verse uses the imperative 'rizquhunna' which implies obligation in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the Qiyas (Analogy)
The text compares child nafaqah to wife's nafaqah. Explain the link: just as no one shares in the wife's maintenance because the marriage is exclusive, similarly no one shares in the child's nafaqah because the child has only one father.
This is qiyas jali (obvious analogy) based on the principle that responsibility follows exclusivity of relationship.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Identify the narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that divides nafaqah according to inheritance shares. Contrast this with the Zahir al-Riwayah position held by the Sahibayn and the relied-upon view.
The mufta bihi is the father's exclusive responsibility, as agreed by the four Imams.
5
State the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
Consider the real-world effect: under the relied-upon view, the mother is never forced to contribute; under the other view, she would have to pay up to one-third.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل. ونفقة الاولاد الصغار على الاب لايشاركه فيها احد كما لايشاركه فى نفقة الزوجة لقوله تعالى وعلى المولودله رزقهن والمولود له هو الاب
Section: The maintenance of minor children is upon the father, no one shares with him in it, just as no one shares with him in the maintenance of the wife, due to the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And upon the father of the child is their provision' (al-Baqarah 2:233). The father of the child is the father.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement with a proof from the Quran. It states that the nafaqah of minor children is the sole responsibility of the father, with no partner sharing this obligation. The reasoning is twofold: (1) The Quranic verse 'Wa 'ala al-mawludi lahu rizquhunna' (Upon the father of the child is their provision) indicates that the father (mawludu lahu) is responsible. (2) By analogy, just as the father alone bears the nafaqah of his wife (no one else is required to contribute), similarly the nafaqah of his children is solely his. This is based on the principle that responsibilities follow the relationship: the wife's nafaqah is exclusive because she cannot be shared (a woman cannot be married to two men at once), and likewise a child cannot have two fathers, so the nafaqah is exclusive. The text also notes that this is the ruling of the Zahir al-Riwayah and is agreed upon by the four Imams. However, there is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that the nafaqah is divided according to inheritance shares (two-thirds on father, one-third on mother), but this is not the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
مولود له (mawludu lahu): The father of the child, the one to whom the child is born.
نفقة الاولاد (nafaqat al-awlad): Maintenance of children, including food, clothing, education, etc.
الصغار (al-sighar): Minor children who are not yet independent or able to earn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, who is responsible for the nafaqah of minor children?
A) Both parents equally.
B) The father alone, with no one sharing.
C) The father and mother according to inheritance shares.
D) The mother until the father returns.

Key Takeaway
The nafaqah of minor children is wajib exclusively on the father, with no sharing, based on Quran and qiyas.

Maternal Breastfeeding Obligation in Hanafi Law
Ruling with proof on maternal breastfeeding obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maternal breastfeeding and apply the exception when a wet nurse is unavailable.

Core Idea
A mother is not forced to breastfeed unless no wet nurse exists.

This lesson examines a key ruling from Al-Hidayah on whether a mother can be compelled to breastfeed her nursing child. The Hanafi school balances the mother's freedom with the child's welfare, drawing on Quranic principles and the father's financial responsibility. Understanding this ruling clarifies the boundaries of parental obligations in Islamic family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is the mother breastfeeding her nursing infant. The default ruling is that she is not obliged to do so (mubah).
The passage begins: 'it is not upon his mother to nurse him'.
2
Examine the first proof: father's responsibility
The text argues that sufficiency (kifayah) is the father's duty, and the wages of nursing are like nafaqah, which he must pay. Therefore, the mother may refuse and let the father hire a wet nurse.
This is a qiyas-based argument: ujrat al-rida' is analogous to nafaqah.
3
Examine the second proof: Quranic interpretation
The verse 'La tudārr wālidatun biwaladihā' is interpreted to prohibit harming the mother by compelling her to nurse when she is unwilling or unable.
The text says 'compelling her to nurse while she dislikes it' is against the verse's spirit.
4
Identify the exception
If no wet nurse is available, the mother is compelled to breastfeed to prevent the child from wasting away (sawanan lil-sabī 'an al-ḍayā).
This exception prioritizes the child's life over the mother's freedom.
5
Synthesize the overall position
Default: mother not compelled (mubah). If no alternative exists, nursing becomes wajib on her, enforced by the court.
This reflects the Hanafi balance between individual rights and necessity (ḍarūrah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ان كان الصغير رضيعا فليس على امه ان ترضعه لما بينا ان الكفاية على الاب واجرة الرضاع كالنفقة ولانها عساها لاتقدر عليه لعذر بها فلا معنى للجبر عليه وقيل فى تاويل قوله تعالى ولاتضار والدة بولدها بالزامها الارضاع مع كراهتها وهذا الذى ذكر نابيان الحكم وذالك اذا كان يوجد من ترضعه اما اذا كان لا توجد من ترضعه تجبر الام على الارضاع صيانة للصبى عن الضياع
And if the minor is a nursing infant, it is not upon his mother to nurse him, because we have established that sufficiency is upon the father, and the wages of nursing are like nafaqah, and because she may not be able to do so due to an excuse, so there is no meaning to compulsion. And it is said in the interpretation of the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And do not harm a mother by her child' (al-Baqarah 2:233) by compelling her to nurse while she dislikes it. And what has been mentioned is an explanation of the ruling. And that is when there is someone to nurse him. But if there is no one to nurse him, the mother is compelled to nurse to save the child from waste.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof discussing whether a mother can be compelled to breastfeed her nursing child. The Hanafi position is that she is not obliged to breastfeed if a wet nurse is available, because the responsibility for the child's maintenance (kifayah) is on the father, and the wages for nursing are akin to nafaqah, which the father must provide. Additionally, the mother may have a valid excuse (e.g., illness) preventing her from nursing, so compulsion would be unreasonable. The Quranic verse 'La tudārr wālidatun biwaladihā' (No mother shall be harmed because of her child) is interpreted to mean that she should not be forced to nurse if it is burdensome. However, this rule applies only when a wet nurse can be found. If no wet nurse is available, then the mother is compelled to breastfeed in order to protect the child from harm (sawanan lil-sabī 'an al-ḍayā'). This shows the Hanafi balance between individual rights and the welfare of the child.

Key Terms
رضاع (ridā‘): Breastfeeding of a nursing infant.
اجرة الرضاع (ujrat al-ridā‘): Wages for breastfeeding, which the father must pay to the wet nurse.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion, forcing someone to do something against their will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is a mother compelled to breastfeed her nursing child?
A) She is always compelled to breastfeed her own child.
B) She is compelled only if the father cannot afford a wet nurse.
C) She is compelled only if no wet nurse is available to save the child from harm.
D) She is never compelled; she has the absolute right to refuse.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes the child's life over the mother's freedom when no alternative provider exists, but otherwise upholds her right to refuse nursing.

The Diyanatan Obligation of Breastfeeding
Distinguishing diyanatan from qada'an in breastfeeding obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the distinction between diyanatan and qada'an in breastfeeding and identify when breastfeeding is wajib diyanatan.

Core Idea
Breastfeeding is a religious duty (wajib diyanatan) but not enforceable by court unless the child's life is at risk.

The passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies a crucial Hanafi principle: obligations are of two types – those binding in conscience before Allah (diyanatan) and those enforceable by a court (qada'an). For breastfeeding, the mother's obligation is diyanatan, meaning she is religiously required to breastfeed under certain conditions, but the court will not compel her unless the child faces imminent harm. This lesson unpacks the conditions of this diyanatan obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed? Breastfeeding (irda'). What is the hukm level? It is wajib diyanatan, not fard. Note the two spheres: diyanatan (religious) and qada'an (court).
Diyanatan means binding in conscience before Allah; qada'an means legally enforceable by a judge.
2
Define diyanatan and qada'an in context
Diyanatan: the mother is religiously obligated to breastfeed when she is married or in 'iddah, not excused. Qada'an: the court will not force her unless an exception applies.
This distinction is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh. Some schools do not distinguish as sharply.
3
State the conditions for diyanatan wajib
The mother must be in a valid marriage or in 'iddah after a revocable divorce. She must have no legitimate excuse (e.g., illness). If these conditions are met, breastfeeding is wajib upon her, and taking payment for it is not permissible.
After 'iddah expires, the obligation lapses; she may demand payment if she breastfeeds.
4
Explain the rule on payment
When breastfeeding is wajib diyanatan (during marriage/'iddah), the mother cannot demand payment; it is her duty. If she refuses even after 'iddah, she may charge a fee.
This preserves the child's right while acknowledging the mother's changed status.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that breastfeeding is wajib and enforceable by court (qada'an) in all cases, not just emergencies. The Hanafi school restricts court compulsion to emergency scenarios only.
The difference stems from different interpretations of the Quranic verse on mutual consultation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب النفقة
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد پنجم بچہ کی وجہ سے اور نہ کسی باپ کو تکلیف دینی چاہئے اس کے بچہ کی وجہ سے۔ کی تفسیر میں کہا گیا کہ اس پر دودھ پلانا لازم نہ کیا جائے گا۔ جبکہ یہ امر اس پر گراں ہو ۔ چنانچہ حضرت اقدس تھانوی نے لکھا ہے کہ بچہ کے ماں باپ آپس میں کسی بات پر ضد اضدی نہ کریں۔ ماں اگر کسی وجہ سے معذور نہ ہو۔ تو اس کے ذمہ دیائیہ یعنی عند اللہ واجب ہے کہ بچہ کو دودھ پلا وے۔ جبکہ وہ منکوحہ ہو یا عدت میں ہو اور اجرت لینا درست نہیں اور اگر طلاق کے بعد عدت گذر چکی تو اس پر بلا اجرت دودھ پلانا واجب نہیں اگر ماں دودھ پلانے سے انکار کرے تو اس پر جبر نہ کیا جائے گا۔ البتہ اگر بچہ کسی کا دودھ ہی نہیں لیتا اور نہ اوپر کا دودھ پیتا ہے تو ماں کو مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ اسی طرح اگر ماں دودھ پلانا چاہتی ہے اور اس کے دودھ میں کوئی خرابی بھی نہیں تو باپ کو جائز نہیں کہ اس کو نہ پلانے دے اور دوسری انا کا دودھ بلوائے ۔ ماں دودھ پلانے پر رضامند ہے۔ لیکن اس کا دودھ بچہ کو مضر ہے تو باپ کو جائز ہے کہ اس کو دودھ پلانے نہ دے اور کسی انا کا دودھ پلوائے اور یہ جو کچھ ہم نے ذکر کیا یعنی ماں کو دودھ پلانے پر مجبور نہ کرنا یہ حکم قضاء کا بیان ہے۔ ورنہ دیانہ ماں پر دودھ پلانا واجب ہے اور ماں پر دودھ پلانے کا واجب نہ ہونا اس وقت تک ہے کہ بچہ کو دودھ پلانے والی میسر ہو اور اگر دودھ پلانے والی میسر نہ ہو یا بچہ کسی عورت کا دودھ نہ لیتا ہو تو اس کی ماں پر دودھ پلانے کے واسطے جبر کیا جائے گا۔ تا کہ بچہ ضائع ہونے سے بچے۔
Chapter of Maintenance. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary volume five: ... neither should the father be troubled because of his child. In its interpretation it was said: breastfeeding will not be made obligatory upon him when this matter is burdensome upon him. Thus Hadrat Thanwi has written: the parents of the child should not be stubborn with each other in any matter. If the mother is not excused for some reason, then upon her it is wajib diyanatan, i.e., in the sight of Allah, to breastfeed the child, while she is married or in 'iddah, and taking payment is not correct. And if after divorce the 'iddah has passed, then breastfeeding without payment is not wajib upon her. If the mother refuses to breastfeed, she will not be forced. However, if the child does not take anyone's milk and does not drink artificial milk, then the mother will be compelled. Similarly, if the mother wishes to breastfeed and there is no defect in her milk, it is not permissible for the father to prevent her and bring another's milk. The mother is willing to breastfeed but her milk is harmful to the child, then it is permissible for the father to prevent her and bring another’s milk. And what we have mentioned, i.e., not forcing the mother to breastfeed, this is the ruling of the court. Otherwise, diyanatan breastfeeding is wajib upon the mother. And the breastfeeding not being wajib upon the mother is only as long as there is a wet nurse available for the child. And if a wet nurse is not available or the child does not take any woman's milk, then the mother will be compelled to breastfeed so that the child is saved from destruction.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage explains the distinction between diyanatan (religious obligation in conscience) and qada'an (court-enforceable ruling) regarding breastfeeding. The author, commenting on the Hidayah, states that the mother is not forced by the court to breastfeed if it is burdensome, but diyanatan it is wajib upon her when she is married or in 'iddah. Taking payment is not allowed when it is wajib. After 'iddah, breastfeeding without payment is not wajib. Exceptions exist: if no other milk source is available, the mother is compelled to save the child. The father cannot prevent breastfeeding if the milk is good, but can if it is harmful. This is consistent with Hanafi fiqh where the court does not enforce supererogatory acts. The ruling is based on the Quranic verse (implied) and the principle of avoiding harm. The authority weight is from the commentary of Ashraf al-Hidayah, which relies on Hidayah (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms
ديانة (diyanatan): In conscience; religiously binding but not enforceable by court.
قضاء (qada'an): By court order; legally enforceable.
إرضاع (irda'): Breastfeeding a child.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion by court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is breastfeeding wajib diyanatan upon the mother?
A) Whenever she is able, regardless of marital status.
B) Only when she is married or in 'iddah and not excused.
C) Only when a wet nurse is unavailable.
D) Whenever the father demands it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation to breastfeed is primarily a religious duty; the court only intervenes when the child's survival is at stake.

Court Enforceability (Qada'an) and Exceptions
Examining when court compulsion applies and the father's rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which the court can compel breastfeeding and apply the exceptions to the general rule of no compulsion.

Core Idea
The court may force the mother to breastfeed only when no other milk source is available and the child would otherwise perish.

While the general rule in the Hanafi school is that the court does not force the mother to breastfeed, there are two exceptional scenarios where compulsion becomes necessary to save the child's life. This lesson examines these exceptions, as well as the father's role in ensuring the child's welfare. Understanding these limits prevents both under- and over-enforcement of breastfeeding obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule on compulsion
The general rule from the passage: 'If the mother refuses to breastfeed, she will not be forced.' This is the qada'an (court) position. The court does not compel the mother as long as a wet nurse is available.
This rule applies unless specific exceptions are met.
2
Identify the first exception
Exception 1: 'If a wet nurse is not available.' That is, no other woman can be found to breastfeed the child. In that case, the mother can be compelled.
The availability of artificial milk (formula) is considered – the passage mentions 'does not drink artificial milk'. So if formula is available and accepted, the exception does not apply.
3
Identify the second exception
Exception 2: 'The child does not take any woman's milk.' Even if a wet nurse is available, if the child refuses all other milk and only accepts the mother's milk, she can be compelled.
This is a case of extreme necessity (darurat).
4
Explain the father's role and rights
The father cannot prevent the mother from breastfeeding if her milk is good and she wants to. However, if her milk is harmful to the child, he may prevent her and hire a wet nurse. This balances the rights of all parties.
The child's health is paramount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Father Hiring a Wet Nurse: Custody and Breastfeeding Location
Ruling with proof from Mukhtasar al-Quduri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the father's obligation to hire a wet nurse and explain why breastfeeding must occur at the mother's home.

Core Idea
Father pays, mother's custody right determines where the wet nurse breastfeeds.

This passage from Mukhtasar al-Quduri clarifies an important intersection of maintenance (nafaqah) and custody (hadana). While the father is financially responsible for hiring a wet nurse, the mother's right to keep the child in her care dictates that the wet nurse must come to her home, unless she agrees otherwise. This ruling protects the mother's bond with the child during the breastfeeding period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
The father, mother, wet nurse, and child. The father pays, the mother has custody, the wet nurse breastfeeds.
2
Determine who bears the cost
The father must hire and pay the wet nurse because maintenance is his responsibility.
3
Locate where breastfeeding occurs
The wet nurse must breastfeed at the mother's home, as per al-Quduri's statement 'at her place'.
4
Analyze the condition of the mother's wish
The location condition applies only if the mother wishes; she can agree to another arrangement.
Custody right (hajr) belongs to the mother, so she controls where the child is raised.
5
Connect to the broader principle of hadana
The mother's right of custody over young children overrides convenience or paternal authority in this matter.
This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi prioritization of maternal custody.
